 Miss        _Frontispiece_
89𓀴             Lestrange,
 “to let my boy go”

 she whispered,
 “I              127

 the risk--if you
93𓀴             loved me”

99𓀴
100𓀴                   
          HELEN OF TROY
101𓀴
102𓀴
103𓀴                
                   I
104𓀴
105𓀴Horace Lestrange
 was intent upon his
 occupation; he was throwing stones
106𓀴into the lake. He
 did it with skill
 and success; he made each stone jump
107𓀴four times, but he was using only
 his outer layer of attention; his
108𓀴inner self was turning
 over and over
 again a personal problem; he would
109𓀴have said he was thinking it out,
 but this was a mistake. The case was
110𓀴very plain and required
 no thought;
 he was only feeling it over, probing
111𓀴sensation to find how much weight
 it would bear, and at what point his
112𓀴heart would cry out
 to him to stop.
 Ten years ago he had lost his wife,
113𓀴after one year’s
 marriage. Perhaps,
 if she had lived, he would have
114𓀴grown tired of her;
 but she was very
 beautiful, and she had died when
115𓀴love was new and every golden day
 of her presence a thing divine and
116𓀴separate, intolerable to lose.
117𓀴
118𓀴She had left him
 something; instead
 of her love and her ripened youth,
119𓀴she had given him
 a baby son; he had
 put the child in his sister’s care
120𓀴and gone abroad.
121𓀴
122𓀴Annette used to go to church very
 devoutly, and Horace went to please
123𓀴her. He tried to suppose
 that Providence
 was in the right, but he said
124𓀴to his oldest friend (and this was
 the only comment he was heard to make
125𓀴upon his grief):
126𓀴
127𓀴“It seems to me, Bambridge,
 love is rather a let in.”
128𓀴
129𓀴Bambridge cleared his
 throat sympathetically.
130𓀴
131𓀴“A deuced lot of things
 are,” he muttered.
132𓀴
133𓀴Annette had always thought
 Bambridge rather cynical.
134𓀴
135𓀴Time heals wounds,
 but it leaves scars.
 Ten years had done a great deal
136𓀴for Horace Lestrange. There was no
 mark of his great grief left; but
137𓀴although time works very well as a
 narcotic, it is not stimulating; it
138𓀴had not renewed Horace’s youth. He
 did not think of love now; he thought
139𓀴of marriage--comfortable,
 consoling marriage.
140𓀴
141𓀴The girl who had
 suggested this idea
 to him was a thoroughly nice girl,
142𓀴pretty, well-educated,
 and kind-hearted.
 She had been very good to
143𓀴Horace; they had rowed on the lake
 together, and her ways were energetic
144𓀴without hardness, and swift with
 grace. They had climbed some of the
145𓀴surrounding peaks side by side,
 and she had shown admirable
146𓀴characteristics--quietness, pluck,
 instantaneous obedience, and
147𓀴endurance. She was
 a good companion
 (she challenged no comparison with
148𓀴Annette, who was
 helpless, clinging,
 and thoroughly silly, the kind of
149𓀴woman whom--if she
 dies soon enough--a
 man never forgets). Edith’s hair
150𓀴and eyes were dark; she had a full
 sweet mouth and a round chin. She was
151𓀴quite thirty and she wouldn’t
 expect romance. . . .
152𓀴
153𓀴The last stone failed to jump four
 times; perhaps it didn’t agree with
154𓀴Horace that there is a
 time limit for romance.
155𓀴
156𓀴“It would be an excellent
 thing for
 the boy,” said Lestrange, putting
157𓀴his hands in his pockets. “Etta
 of course is a good woman; clever,
158𓀴plenty of tact, but
 she is so managing.
 I never knew such a woman; she
159𓀴sponges one up. She
 has been everything
 to the little chap though for
160𓀴the last eight years.
 I hope he won’t
 make a fuss at leaving her. I
161𓀴don’t think he will; Edith is good
 with kids. Well, I’ll go and look her
162𓀴up.”
163𓀴
164𓀴He went to look her
 up. She was usually
 easy to discover when Lestrange
165𓀴wanted her. She did not run after
 him, as a sillier woman would have
166𓀴done, neither did
 she run away from
 him, as young girls sometimes run
167𓀴from their lovers,
 but when he looked
 for her she was there. She sat
168𓀴under a big ilex
 tree on the terrace
 of the hotel garden. It was not
169𓀴easy to remember that it was an
 hotel, for once it had been an old
170𓀴Italian palace, and
 something of its
 ancient dignity remained. The lake
171𓀴lay at its foot, a
 vast and shimmering
 expanse of silver and azure. The
172𓀴mountains were half withdrawn into
 vague shadows; sometimes moonstone
173𓀴and sometimes purple, and when the
 wind blew aside their thin veil of
174𓀴mist, the sun shone over slopes
 vivid, luminous and green.
175𓀴
176𓀴Around Edith Walton
 were huge bushes
 of camellias, red and white and
177𓀴very splendid. A mad riot of roses
 flung itself over a pergola. In the
178𓀴distance a magnolia tree slowly
 opened wonderful flowers to the
179𓀴sun--flowers that seemed like the
 birth of spiritual treasures, white
180𓀴cloistered buds filled
 with aromatic fragrance.
181𓀴
182𓀴Edith sat quite still
 with her hands
 in her lap; there was something
183𓀴expectant in her appearance;
 it seemed
 part of the general hush. The
184𓀴wind had dropped suddenly and the
 tiny village lay embosomed on
185𓀴quivering water lines.
186𓀴
187𓀴Edith knew who was coming towards
 her, as flowers know the quickening
188𓀴soft rain of spring,
 and as the ocean
 knows the dominance of the tides.
189𓀴
190𓀴“You’ve got an awfully
 jolly corner,”
 said Lestrange rather awkwardly.
191𓀴
192𓀴“There are so many awfully jolly
 corners here,” said Edith. Then she
193𓀴smiled at him, the tender smile of
 a woman who laughs in secret triumph
194𓀴at the man she loves; she lets him
 think he is concealing his purpose
195𓀴from her, but she smiles.
196𓀴
197𓀴“I wish you weren’t
 going away to-morrow,”
 Lestrange began. He thought
198𓀴he was leading up to his goal with
 extraordinary skill and subtlety.
199𓀴“Must you really?”
200𓀴
201𓀴Edith hesitated; he would have
 to put it better than that.
202𓀴
203𓀴“I think my aunt has made all her
 arrangements,” she said. Then she
204𓀴looked away towards the lake over
 his shoulder. “I shall be sorry to
205𓀴leave--all this,” she
 murmured quietly.
206𓀴
207𓀴“I don’t see why
 you can’t stay with
 me,” said Lestrange, sitting down
208𓀴on the seat beside
 her. “I mean always.”
209𓀴
210𓀴He was certainly
 not putting it very
 well. Edith tried to believe that
211𓀴he was; she wanted to believe it.
 She looked at him, and her lips
212𓀴quivered. She was not an emotional
 woman; he had taken good care to find
213𓀴that out, but her dark eyes looked
 strange and stormy. She seemed as if
214𓀴she was feeling something
 strongly,
 almost more than she could bear.
215𓀴
216𓀴“Do you want me very
 much?” she murmured.
217𓀴
218𓀴Well, of course he
 wanted her. If you
 ask a woman to marry you, you must
219𓀴want her unless you
 are a young fool
 under the influence of glamour.
220𓀴There was no glamour.
 Horace had never
 pretended in his life, and he did
221𓀴not pretend now. He simply said:
222𓀴
223𓀴“It would make me very happy if
 you would be my wife, Edith.”
224𓀴
225𓀴“I should like so much to make you
 very happy,” said Edith. Then,
226𓀴suddenly, inconsequently, and very
 foolishly, she burst into tears.
227𓀴
228𓀴“Don’t, my dear--don’t,”
 he exclaimed
 hurriedly. He tried to take her
229𓀴hands from her face, but she would
 not let him. He looked at her in
230𓀴bewilderment; she shook with these
 astonishing sobs; and she was a most
231𓀴sensible woman, and thirty.
 He could not understand her.
232𓀴
233𓀴He kissed the clenched hands which
 covered her face, and almost as
234𓀴suddenly the sobs ceased. She drew
 in her breath with a quick sound. He
235𓀴walked to the balustrade and began
 to whistle. They were in a very
236𓀴secluded part of the
 gardens, but one
 never knew. No! fortunately there
237𓀴was no one in sight.
 What an extraordinary,
 lovely scene it was! Perhaps
238𓀴Edith would stop crying soon.
239𓀴
240𓀴She did; she brushed the tears
 from her eyes and laughed.
241𓀴
242𓀴“Oh, how silly you must think me!”
 she said. “And I’m thirty--you know
243𓀴I’m thirty?”
244𓀴
245𓀴“I think this is
 the third time that
 you have told me you are,” cried
246𓀴Horace. He came and
 sat down beside
 her again. She did not make him feel
247𓀴uncomfortable any more.
248𓀴
249𓀴“Do smoke,” said Edith quickly.
 “I know you’re dying to.”
250𓀴
251𓀴“Thanks, if I may.”
 He lit; a cigarette.
 And she saw with a sudden
252𓀴sinking of her heart that
 his hands were steady.
253𓀴
254𓀴“There is the little
 chap at home,”
 he said, turning his eyes to her
255𓀴with a restored friendliness.
 “You’re
 sure you won’t mind him?”
256𓀴
257𓀴“Oh, I shall love
 him!” said Edith.
 “Do you know--you must not be
258𓀴jealous, but that
 is half the reason
 why I am so--why I am going to
259𓀴marry you, you know!”
260𓀴
261𓀴Horace was not jealous. He was
 very pleased, and he said so.
262𓀴
263𓀴“But what,” Edith asked anxiously,
 “will your sister say, Horace?”
264𓀴
265𓀴“Oh, my sister!” stammered
 Lestrange.
 “Do you think she will mind very
266𓀴much?”
267𓀴
268𓀴“You darling stupid!” cried Edith.
 “She’ll mind most horribly.”
269𓀴
270𓀴Then she blushed; she hadn’t meant
 to call him “darling.” She looked at
271𓀴him anxiously, but he
 had not noticed it.
272𓀴
273𓀴“By Jove, I believe
 she will; you’re
 right, Edith. I’m afraid she’ll cut
274𓀴up frightfully rough!
 I thought I had
 managed to think it all out--about
275𓀴you, you know, and the little chap
 and me--and Annette, my dead wife.”
276𓀴
277𓀴He spoke these last
 words in a voice
 she had never heard him use before.
278𓀴But he spoke them bravely
 and honestly,
 with his eyes on hers. Her
279𓀴courage leapt to meet his.
280𓀴
281𓀴“My dear,” she said
 quickly, “I want
 you to behave as if I had loved her
282𓀴too. I want you to talk to me of
 her, to let her share our life, or
283𓀴rather to let me share yours and
 hers. I want you never to be afraid
284𓀴that I do not understand. I come
 to you to give you all the help and
285𓀴comfort that I can,
 but I come to you
 knowing that she has your heart.”
286𓀴
287𓀴Then Lestrange kissed
 her. The last
 hesitation fell away from this new
288𓀴purpose, the last
 cloud melted. His
 heart went out in friendship and
289𓀴gratitude to this
 woman who did not
 seek to rob him of his past. There
290𓀴was a moment’s splendid
 recognition
 between them, as strong as passion
291𓀴and as kind as love.
 Then the breathless
 hush of the air broke in a
292𓀴chill and sudden
 shower; they passed
 through the drenched garden quickly
293𓀴into the big hotel.
294𓀴
295𓀴
296𓀴                 
                  II
297𓀴
298𓀴When Miss Lestrange received the
 announcement of her brother’s
299𓀴engagement she replied by return
 of post, congratulating him on his
300𓀴prospective happiness. She called
 it prospective, but she allowed that
301𓀴it was happiness. She offered to
 give up her residence at Mallows,
302𓀴Horace’s place in the country, and
 suggested that perhaps she could take
303𓀴a villa by the seaside.
304𓀴
305𓀴“This,” she wrote, “would
 be very suitable
 for Leslie as well.” She took
306𓀴entirely for granted
 that she should
 keep the boy. Then she said to
307𓀴herself: “Edith Walton!
 What extraordinary
 people Horace picks up! One
308𓀴has never heard of her! There was
 a Lady Walton I remember meeting at
309𓀴Bournemouth; her husband probably
 got knighted for patent biscuits, or
310𓀴some vulgar charity; the Lindleys
 knew her. I will call on the
311𓀴Lindleys.”
312𓀴
313𓀴When Horace returned to London
 he found his home, as usual, the
314𓀴perfection of order.
 He was not a rich
 man, and he did not desire luxury
315𓀴or extravagance.
 He had never needed
 to desire them, for his sister had
316𓀴that genius for management
 which results
 in other people’s comfort. She
317𓀴oiled the wheels of
 life for her brother,
 and as yet she had charged him
318𓀴nothing for the oil.
 She was dressed
 for going out, but on his arrival
319𓀴she laid her card-case and parasol
 on the table and gave him her cheek
320𓀴to kiss. Miss Lestrange had been a
 plain, angular girl, without charms;
321𓀴but she was a di
stinguished-looking
 middle-aged woman with a pleasant
322𓀴manner. Her pleasant
 manner entirely
 hid from the world that she had a
323𓀴will of iron and an
 absorbing passion
 for her little nephew. She was
324𓀴famous for her kind
 heart, and made
 an excellent _confidante_; people
325𓀴talked of her as “a
 dear, kind old thing.”
326𓀴
327𓀴Her brother looked at
 her a little nervously.
328𓀴
329𓀴“You have received
 my letter?” said
 Miss Lestrange, sitting down again,
330𓀴and drawing on her
 gloves. “But, of
 course, there is a great deal to be
331𓀴talked over, isn’t
 there? We needn’t
 begin now. Do you want a meal or
332𓀴anything? Or do they
 give it to you
 on the train? It is so long since I
333𓀴have been on the Continent,
 but I understand
 that in America you can be
334𓀴shaved and have your
 corns cut, probably
 simultaneously, as you travel.”
335𓀴
336𓀴“I think I’ll ring for tea,” said
 Horace. “Where is the boy?”
337𓀴
338𓀴“I am afraid he is out
 with Mr. Flinders.
 I should have kept him in, of
339𓀴course, to meet you;
 but Mr. Flinders
 said it was such a perfect
340𓀴afternoon it seemed a pity to keep
 him in, and I never like to interfere
341𓀴with the tutor’s arrangements.
 Leslie sent you his love.”
342𓀴
343𓀴“Thanks,” said Horace,
 a little dryly.
 He fidgeted about the room; he
344𓀴hardly knew quite what he expected
 from Etta, but, by Jove! she needn’t
345𓀴go on smoothing her
 gloves--it made
 him feel cold between the shoulders.
346𓀴
347𓀴“I hope the cake
 is not heavy,” said
 his sister, rising to pour out his
348𓀴tea. “Mrs. Devon can’t
 make cakes--it
 is her only weakness; but there
349𓀴are some rather nice
 pink things over
 there from the confectioner’s.”
350𓀴
351𓀴Horace cleared his throat.
352𓀴
353𓀴“I wish you would take off your
 things, Etta,” he said with sudden
354𓀴irritation, “or not look as if you
 were being kept in by force, and
355𓀴meant to go out the
 moment I had swallowed
 my tea; it makes me nervous.”
356𓀴
357𓀴“Nervous, my dear
 boy? Lestranges are
 never nervous. What is the matter
358𓀴with you? I was going out calling,
 and I supposed you would want to go
359𓀴upstairs and tidy
 after your journey.
 But, of course, if you are
360𓀴nervous, and have anything
 on your mind--”
361𓀴
362𓀴Etta began unbuttoning her
 gloves. Her brother groaned.
363𓀴
364𓀴“No, hang it all, Etta; I’d rather
 wait till after dinner!”
365𓀴
366𓀴“Just as you like,”
 said Miss Lestrange.
 “I hope Miss--er--Walton, isn’t
367𓀴it?--is quite well?”
368𓀴
369𓀴“Oh, yes, Edith is
 all right, thanks.
 You might tell them, Etta, to let
370𓀴the little chap come into my study
 when he gets in from his walk.”
371𓀴
372𓀴“Oh, of course, Horace!”
373𓀴
374𓀴It may have been intention, or it
 may have been one of those fortunate
375𓀴accidents which happen
 to well-trained
 fighters, but Miss Lestrange’s
376𓀴attention was suddenly caught by a
 crooked picture. She turned back to a
377𓀴portrait of Annette hanging
 over the mantelpiece.
378𓀴
379𓀴“It is not hung quite
 straight,” she
 said in her pleasant, commonplace
380𓀴voice. “There, that’s
 better! Is there
 anything more you want, Horace?”
381𓀴
382𓀴Her brother’s answer was made from
 his teacup; it sounded very like
383𓀴“Damn!”
384𓀴
385𓀴Horace continued to be extremely
 nervous. He had meant to go and see
386𓀴Edith after dinner;
 she and her aunt,
 Lady Walton, had returned to town
387𓀴with him, but he couldn’t
 go to Edith,
 having arranged nothing whatever,
388𓀴and not even having mentioned that
 he intended to keep his boy.
389𓀴
390𓀴All through dinner Etta held the
 conversation and guided it as she
391𓀴chose. Mr. Flinders, the
 tutor, responded admirably.
392𓀴
393𓀴Miss Lestrange had a perfect tone
 with the tutor; she treated him with
394𓀴that deference which
 marks the difference
 in social value. Her delicate
395𓀴flattery was a restraint; it put
 him at once on the footing of an
396𓀴inferior position where
 she could afford
 to be delightful to him without
397𓀴his ever meeting her on her
 own level. It was too fine for
398𓀴condescension, too gracious for
 patronage; it was an “invulnerable
399𓀴nothing”; and yet
 you could no more
 have passed it than have walked
400𓀴through bayonets;
 and there was this
 added attraction, that the bayonets
401𓀴were garlanded with flowers.
402𓀴
403𓀴“It was such a pity Leslie behaved
 so badly this afternoon,” Miss
404𓀴Lestrange began.
 “Mr. Flinders felt
 that such direct disobedience must
405𓀴be punished, especially
 when it led
 to such a decided risk as the boy’s
406𓀴playing with whooping-cough
 children
 in the Park. So instead of being
407𓀴allowed to come and
 see you, Horace,
 he had to be packed off,
408𓀴supperless, to bed; but you will
 go up after dinner, I suppose?”
409𓀴
410𓀴“I should have gone up
 before,” interrupted
 Horace, “but they said--”
411𓀴
412𓀴“Yes, I think authority
 must be upheld,”
 said his sister. “You see, dear
413𓀴Horace, Mr. Flinders
 had already warned
 Leslie about the punishment.”
414𓀴
415𓀴She looked across at her brother,
 as if to say that the punishment was
416𓀴an absurd blunder of Mr. Flinders,
 which they must overlook, because
417𓀴although he was a
 very clever fellow,
 of course, and a clergyman’s son,
418𓀴and really quite a
 gentleman, still--
419𓀴
420𓀴Horace understood the
 look, and dropped
 the subject. He was a man who
421𓀴took almost everything
 very easily;
 but not quite everything.
422𓀴
423𓀴Mr. Flinders began to make some
 explanation, which Miss Lestrange
424𓀴promptly checked.
 She asked his advice
 about a book, and somehow or
425𓀴other Leslie’s punishment remained
 the tutor’s blunder, though this was
426𓀴the first time he
 had ever heard of
 it. He had probably misunderstood
427𓀴something Miss Lestrange had said
 to him; she had often observed that
428𓀴she was not a lucid talker; there
 were certain advantages which Mr.
429𓀴Flinders had had, and she had not,
 and this made it such a comfort to
430𓀴listen to him! Possibly this was
 one of the occasions in which the
431𓀴disadvantages had told.
432𓀴
433𓀴After dinner Horace went upstairs
 to see his boy; there were traces of
434𓀴tears on the child’s face, and he
 looked pathetically like his dead
435𓀴mother. He flung his arms around
 his father’s neck and began to sob.
436𓀴Leslie had inherited Annette’s
 weak constitution; he was a
437𓀴highly-strung, delicate
 little boy.
438𓀴
439𓀴“Oh, daddy, don’t--don’t--don’t!”
 he sobbed. “Oh, please, dear daddy,
440𓀴don’t! I will be good
 if you won’t marry her!”
441𓀴
442𓀴His father’s face
 grew suddenly very
 stern; he had meant to be the first
443𓀴to tell his son about Edith.
444𓀴
445𓀴“My dear old chap,”
 he said tenderly,
 sitting down on the bed beside the
446𓀴boy. “Edith is such a jolly girl;
 you will like her. Why, she’s pretty
447𓀴and kind, and awfully
 fond of boys!
 You have no idea what fun we’ll
448𓀴have. She has asked you to go
 with her to-morrow to the Zoo.”
449𓀴
450𓀴“I have been to the
 Zoo,” said Leslie.
451𓀴
452𓀴A firm little line came around his
 mouth. It used to come round his
453𓀴mother’s when she meant to get her
 way and she did not find it easy.
454𓀴Horace had not seen it often
 enough to remember it.
455𓀴
456𓀴“Oh, daddy, don’t make me go with
 her; I want to go away with
457𓀴auntie--oh, I do want
 to go away with
 auntie!” The sobs began to shake
458𓀴him again. “I have
 always had auntie,”
 he cried. “You see, daddy, I’ve
459𓀴always had auntie!”
460𓀴
461𓀴“But, boy, you don’t want to go
 away from me?” asked his father.
462𓀴
463𓀴There was a moment’s constrained
 silence, and then the child dragged
464𓀴himself out of his
 father’s arms and
 threw himself face downwards on his
465𓀴pillow.
466𓀴
467𓀴“Yes, I do,” he muttered
 petulantly.
 “I won’t stay with this new woman!
468𓀴I do want to go away!”
469𓀴
470𓀴The lines of pain on Horace’s face
 deepened. His heart seemed to
471𓀴contract as he looked
 at the golden
 curls on the pillow, and remembered
472𓀴those long golden
 curls he had played
 with and kissed. For a moment he
473𓀴turned away, regretful, sick, and
 undesirous as the child himself of
474𓀴“this new woman.” Then his manhood
 reasserted itself, and he remembered
475𓀴that this was after all only a
 childish fit of ignorant tears.
476𓀴
477𓀴He was not angry
 with the child; it
 did not occur to him to ask him who
478𓀴had given him this
 cruel fear of Edith.
 There were a good many things
479𓀴that never occurred
 to Horace Lestrange.
 They might have been convenient
480𓀴things to do; possibly
 they might have
 made life easy and happy for him,
481𓀴only he did not do
 them, that was all;
 he could not make the child tell
482𓀴tales.
483𓀴
484𓀴There was some one
 to be very angry
 with; that was a simplification. It
485𓀴might be Etta, but Lestrange was
 slow to think so. Hadn’t she
486𓀴congratulated him at
 once? And besides,
 he couldn’t think that Etta
487𓀴could poison a child’s
 mind. Perhaps
 it was that fool Flinders; he
488𓀴seemed a perfectly
 incompetent chap,
 and he might possibly have some
489𓀴sentimental theories
 on step-mothers.
 Anyhow, he would go downstairs and
490𓀴talk to Etta; meanwhile he stooped
 over the child and shook his shoulder
491𓀴gently.
492𓀴
493𓀴“Don’t cry, old man,”
 he said quietly.
 “I promise you, you will like
494𓀴this new friend. She doesn’t want
 to take your mother’s place, or
495𓀴anything; she is
 just a new friend.
 To-morrow you shall see her, and
496𓀴tell me what you feel. You needn’t
 go to the Zoo. Aunt Etta isn’t going
497𓀴away at present, and you shall
 see her whenever you like.”
498𓀴
499𓀴“Mr. Flinders said there was going
 to be great changes,” sobbed the boy.
500𓀴
501𓀴The father closed
 his lips suddenly;
 there was going to be one great
502𓀴change--and that would
 be Mr. Flinders.
 He recalled his sister’s glance
503𓀴at dinner; evidently Etta thought
 the man a fool too. He felt vaguely
504𓀴relieved to have found
 out that it was Flinders.
505𓀴
506𓀴
507𓀴                 
                 III
508𓀴
509𓀴Etta was sitting in
 the library doing
 church embroidery on a frame; it
510𓀴was a thing she did
 extremely well;
 in fact, she was a woman who never
511𓀴did anything badly; if
 there were possibilities
 of ignorance in her, she
512𓀴avoided those fields in which they
 might be betrayed. Horace did not
513𓀴want to talk to her
 while she worked;
 he was never quite sure that he
514𓀴had her whole attention; she might
 be counting stitches or planning
515𓀴patterns, and so
 miss his points. He
 knew, however, that it does not do
516𓀴to start an important conversation
 with a woman by establishing a
517𓀴grievance, so he
 did not ask her to
 stop; he merely found refuge in a
518𓀴succession of cigarettes.
519𓀴
520𓀴“Was it a surprise
 to you, Etta,” he
 began in an off-hand tone, “to hear
521𓀴of my engagement?”
522𓀴
523𓀴Etta took up a thread
 of pink silk,
 and then decided for pale blue.
524𓀴
525𓀴“I don’t know that
 engagements ever
 surprise me,” she replied. “If men
526𓀴can afford to marry, and there is
 no other impediment, they generally
527𓀴do; and if they are attractive
 to women, they always do.”
528𓀴
529𓀴“You mean if women attract
 them?” he interjected.
530𓀴
531𓀴“My dear boy,” said
 his sister, moving
 the frame slightly more under the
532𓀴electric light, “it never does to
 confuse cause and effect. If women
533𓀴want to marry a man,
 he marries. In
 your case, of course, there was some
534𓀴protection provided
 you remained at
 home; the rest was merely a question
535𓀴of time.”
536𓀴
537𓀴Horace did not like
 this way of putting
 the matter at all; in the first
538𓀴place, it was an insult to Edith,
 and in the second place it was an
539𓀴insult to his own intelligence. He
 had thought the thing out so often,
540𓀴and had acted so
 entirely as a free
 agent; and yet the more emphasis he
541𓀴laid on this fact,
 the more plainly
 he saw the pleasant, unconvinced
542𓀴smile upon his sister’s
 face; besides,
 it wasn’t the point of the
543𓀴discussion; they seemed
 strangely incapable
 of reaching the point of the
544𓀴discussion.
545𓀴
546𓀴“I am sure when you see Edith,” he
 said at last, “you will feel that I
547𓀴have made a most
 desirable choice.”
 He tried to put it as baldly as
548𓀴possible, for he did not wish Etta
 to think he had been swayed by
549𓀴glamour.
550𓀴
551𓀴“Walton!” said his sister slowly:
 “who are the Waltons? Has the yellow
552𓀴silk skein fallen at
 your feet, Horace?”
553𓀴
554𓀴“I don’t know that
 they are anybody
 in particular,” said Horace, vaguely
555𓀴uncomfortable; “she’s
 an orphan, you
 know, and Lady Walton, her aunt, is
556𓀴an extremely clever,
 amusing woman.
 Edith has not gone in for Society
557𓀴much, she’s so fond of travel, and
 her aunt’s rather an invalid, so I
558𓀴imagine they have always
 lived extremely quietly.”
559𓀴
560𓀴“I can’t remember,”
 said Etta, “whether
 it was biscuits or soap the
561𓀴Lindleys told me; perhaps
 it was soap.”
562𓀴
563𓀴“What was soap?” said Horace,
 now thoroughly irritated.
564𓀴
565𓀴“What the man Walton,
 you know, was
 knighted for,” said his sister,
566𓀴calmly stitching at a wild rose.
 “Is she a lady by birth?”
567𓀴
568𓀴“The question did not arise,” said
 Horace rather grimly, “and if such
569𓀴questions do not arise,
 the references
 are usually satisfactory.”
570𓀴
571𓀴“Usually,” agreed his sister,
 “but not always, Horace.”
572𓀴
573𓀴“You speak in a very
 mysterious way,
 my dear. May I ask if you have a
574𓀴secret up your sleeve--what
 do they
 call those things in the ‘Family
575𓀴Herald’?--‘an ugly
 secret.’ Have you
 discovered that Lady Walton’s name
576𓀴was Smith?”
577𓀴
578𓀴“I don’t know what
 her name was,” said
 Miss Lestrange, and for a moment
579𓀴she pushed the screen
 away from her.
 “The whole family seem slightly
580𓀴obscure, but I supposed
 Miss Walton’s
 aunt could hardly be a person of
581𓀴much discrimination
 (I am sure I can
 be revealing no secret to you, my
582𓀴dear Horace, as you must know all
 about the thing already)--but how
583𓀴could any one who was a lady allow
 her niece to compromise herself quite
584𓀴as madly on the eve
 of her first London
 season? People of our sort don’t
585𓀴do that kind of thing.”
586𓀴
587𓀴“Pray explain yourself,
 Etta,” said
 Horace, getting up and standing in
588𓀴front of the mantelpiece, where he
 could look down on his sister. “I
589𓀴know nothing of what
 you say. Perhaps
 you have heard some malicious or
590𓀴stupid gossip which
 it is your duty
 to tell me, and mine to contradict.”
591𓀴
592𓀴“I hardly think you
 could do anything
 so foolish as to contradict
593𓀴gossip, my dear Horace, unless
 you wish to revive it; but I will
594𓀴certainly tell you
 what the Lindleys
 told me, and doubtless Edith will
595𓀴find it easy to explain.
 She was found
 staying on the Lake of Como--at
596𓀴the same place, I believe, where
 your engagement took place--with a
597𓀴disreputable woman--a woman about
 whose career there was no shadow of
598𓀴doubt. The Lindleys knew all about
 her, and this woman and Miss Walton
599𓀴were requested to leave the hotel.
 The peculiar part of the whole story
600𓀴is that the aunt and Miss Walton’s
 maid left previously, having
601𓀴apparently discovered
 the character
 of Miss Walton’s companion, and
602𓀴leaving the niece alone with her.
 I told the Lindleys, of course, that
603𓀴there must be some
 perfectly obvious
 explanation, but the fact remains
604𓀴the girl never did come out, and
 that she and her aunt have traveled
605𓀴about more or less ever since.
 I am, I must confess, a little
606𓀴disappointed that you have not
 got an explanation for me.”
607𓀴
608𓀴“There will be no difficulty about
 that,” said Horace quietly.
609𓀴
610𓀴“None, of course,” said his sister
 in courteous agreement. Then there
611𓀴was a pause.
612𓀴
613𓀴Etta continued to
 embroider, but she
 felt flushed and uncomfortable. So
614𓀴far she had simply skirmished; the
 real battle lay ahead. She had
615𓀴counted on her brother opening the
 subject, but he opened nothing. He
616𓀴stood before the
 closed door of her
 future apparently with far more
617𓀴comfort and unconcern than she
 did. Even a clever woman is at a
618𓀴disadvantage with
 a silent man; she
 has no weapon to pierce his armor.
619𓀴Her final onslaught
 had not disconcerted
 him so much as she had hoped.
620𓀴Evidently she was going to have to
 deal with an intelligent woman; no
621𓀴mere fool could have
 won such entire
 confidence from her brother, and
622𓀴without any of the distortions of
 love. Miss Lestrange saw perfectly
623𓀴well that Horace was not in love
 with the girl; she had guessed this
624𓀴from his letter--but she knew it
 the moment she saw him. It gave her
625𓀴unconcealed satisfaction,
 but at the
 same time it was puzzling that he
626𓀴seemed unshaken after her little
 story; she was certain of all the
627𓀴facts. She knew the importance of
 the unembellished, and she never
628𓀴risked an exaggeration
 with her brother.
 Lestranges did not understand
629𓀴exaggeration--at least, the male
 branch never did; if they found you
630𓀴inaccurate they had
 a tiresome habit
 of never accepting what you said
631𓀴without proof. Horace
 had never found
 Etta inaccurate; he had only once
632𓀴or twice thought she was mistaken.
633𓀴
634𓀴Miss Lestrange fidgeted for
 a few minutes, then she said:
635𓀴
636𓀴“Do you think that
 a woman, however
 innocent, who is under such a cloud,
637𓀴is fit to be in the position
 of mother to Annette’s boy?”
638𓀴
639𓀴“I will make every
 inquiry,” said Horace
 reflectively, “and, by the bye,
640𓀴Etta, Flinders must go. I
 don’t approve of Flinders.”
641𓀴
642𓀴“I think myself,” said Etta, “that
 he has taken rather too much upon his
643𓀴shoulders lately.
 You see, you were
 so long abroad, and yet you were his
644𓀴master. Whereas I was hardly in
 a position to dictate to him.”
645𓀴
646𓀴“I shall speak to him
 to-night,” said Horace.
647𓀴
648𓀴Miss Lestrange put
 down her embroidery
 and faced her brother.
649𓀴
650𓀴“Horace,” she said,
 “I hope you found
 Leslie reconciled to the idea of
651𓀴this great change?
 I did not like to
 speak to him about it myself. I am
652𓀴not an emotional woman,
 but my feelings
 for you and for your boy have
653𓀴been very strong. I did not trust
 myself to say much. I told Mr.
654𓀴Flinders that nothing must be said
 to prejudice him against his future
655𓀴step-mother, and then I left
 the subject to you to explain.”
656𓀴
657𓀴“He does not seem to have carried
 out your orders, Etta.”
658𓀴
659𓀴“Oh, my dear Horace,”
 she cried with
 a sudden note of anxiety in her
660𓀴voice, “how dreadful--how
 _very_ dreadful!”
661𓀴
662𓀴“It is exceedingly
 tiresome, of course,
 but I fancy the boy will soon
663𓀴take to Edith; she is
 clever with children.”
664𓀴
665𓀴Miss Lestrange rose to her feet;
 she looked agitated, plain, and
666𓀴awkward; her hands trembled and
 she gathered her sewing materials
667𓀴together. (There was the making
 of an excellent actress about Miss
668𓀴Lestrange.)
669𓀴
670𓀴“My dear boy,” she
 said solicitously,
 “I haven’t liked to bother you
671𓀴about it while you
 were away--these
 things are so intangible--but Dr.
672𓀴Bossage isn’t quite
 pleased with Leslie’s
 health, his constitution is so
673𓀴delicate; he takes after Annette.
 You know I have been almost
674𓀴excessively careful
 of him. I spoke
 to Bossage last week about the
675𓀴impending change,
 and he said it would
 be a very serious matter unless
676𓀴the boy really took
 to her. I blame
 myself, Horace, for not having
677𓀴conquered my feelings
 and spoken to
 Leslie strongly in her favor; but
678𓀴the Lestranges have
 always been sincere;
 there was this story against
679𓀴her. I was too cautious. I waited.
 I am afraid I may have done
680𓀴incalculable harm--”
 She stopped breathless.
 Horace eyed her gravely.
681𓀴
682𓀴“Is that all?” he asked
 as she finished.
683𓀴
684𓀴“You had better go and see Bossage
 yourself,” said Miss Lestrange; “he
685𓀴strongly advised my taking the boy
 to live in the country or by the sea
686𓀴for a year or two, till he becomes
 definitely strong. I daresay you
687𓀴remember my having mentioned it to
 you in my last letter? Of course,
688𓀴should you think it
 best, I will take
 him with pleasure. I have already
689𓀴told you that I will
 send to Mallows
 for all my little things.”
690𓀴
691𓀴“You know you needn’t
 do that, Etta;
 Mallows is as much your home as
692𓀴mine. Edith and I
 will run down when
 we like, but I most certainly wish
693𓀴you to remain there,”
 interrupted her brother.
694𓀴
695𓀴Miss Lestrange bowed her head.
696𓀴
697𓀴“That is like your
 generosity, Horace,”
 she replied slowly. “I accept.
698𓀴Now I am sure you wish to go and
 see Edith before it is too late.”
699𓀴(“Edith” was a distinct
 concession,
 but Miss Lestrange knew the value of
700𓀴inconclusive concessions.)
 “By-and-by
 you will tell me what you two are
701𓀴going to do about the boy. I hope,
 even if you decide to disregard
702𓀴Bossage, you will
 let him come away
 with me after your marriage, till
703𓀴you get settled, and
 it is convenient
 for you to have him back.”
704𓀴
705𓀴After all, she hadn’t put him in a
 corner--she hadn’t tied him down nor
706𓀴asked him for a promise, or made
 a scene. She had done none of the
707𓀴things he had feared;
 she had merely
 given him “rope enough to hang
708𓀴himself,” and then let him go
 to accomplish the performance.
709𓀴
710𓀴Horace did not know
 what had happened;
 he felt, indeed, vaguely
711𓀴uncomfortable. There
 was the strange
 story about Edith--pure folly but
712𓀴still strange--and there was this
 news of his boy’s health and his
713𓀴evident frightened
 horror of the new
 relationship. He might go and see
714𓀴Bossage, but he had
 a horror of going
 to see doctors--a horror born of
715𓀴terrible useless hours,
 while hideous,
 unavailing efforts were being
716𓀴made to stop the feeble ebbing of
 Annette’s little life. No, he wasn’t
717𓀴going to see doctors!
 But Edith was
 so keen to have the little chap. It
718𓀴was hard on Edith.
 (He did not consider
 it was hard upon himself--he was
719𓀴not apt to take that
 view of misfortune.)
 They had talked about him for
720𓀴hours, and it had
 all been so natural
 and right and easy--their future
721𓀴life together had
 been built around
 Annette’s son; the picture seemed
722𓀴suddenly a piece of vacant canvas
 brushed out by ineffaceable hygienic
723𓀴whitewash.
724𓀴
725𓀴There was only one thing to be
 done. He would dismiss Flinders.
726𓀴
727𓀴Mr. Flinders had,
 perhaps, some right
 to consider himself in after years
728𓀴an ill-used man. He had been given
 notice with implacable and relentless
729𓀴abruptness; no explanation
 had been
 given or listened to. If Lestrange
730𓀴had not been so extremely
 quiet, Mr.
 Flinders would have thought he was
731𓀴dealing with a man who
 was in a dangerous
 rage; as it was, he merely
732𓀴clung to the idea (which was not
 originally perhaps his own) that
733𓀴Lestrange was a well-meaning fool,
 governed by a tyrannical and scheming
734𓀴adventuress.
735𓀴
736𓀴Miss Lestrange, who
 was most sympathetic
 about it next morning, assured
737𓀴him of the fact.
738𓀴
739𓀴“It is natural,” she
 said graciously,
 “for people like yourself, Mr.
740𓀴Flinders, with your
 high ideals and
 great independence of spirit, to be
741𓀴surprised at such strong
 and regrettable
 influence wielded over a man
742𓀴like my brother,
 but the Lestranges
 are well known to be, as a family,
743𓀴very susceptible
 to women. I regret
 your going extremely. I spoke to my
744𓀴brother for you, but I am sorry to
 say I found him quite intractable on
745𓀴that and many other
 subjects. You must
 write and let me know how you are
746𓀴getting on. Life is so
 difficult, isn’t it?”
747𓀴
748𓀴Miss Lestrange was
 fond of speaking
 of Life or Destiny as being gigantic
749𓀴monsters with invincible powers,
 and yet there were times when she
750𓀴manipulated these
 great forces very
 easily. Mr. Flinders left her more
751𓀴struck than ever by
 her genuine qualities.
752𓀴
753𓀴“I daresay she will miss me too,”
 he said to himself with pleasant
754𓀴regretfulness. “I may have been of
 some use to her,” and there was no
755𓀴doubt that in this
 particular deduction
 Mr. Flinders was right.
756𓀴
757𓀴
758𓀴                 
                  IV
759𓀴
760𓀴Lady Walton was a woman who never
 did anything with her hands. She was
761𓀴content to sit for hours at a time
 thinking--“doing nothing,” her
762𓀴acquaintances called it. Certain
 hours in the day she read, but she
763𓀴never opened a modern
 book of any kind.
764𓀴
765𓀴“I have a feeling,”
 she said to her
 niece, “that they would revive a
766𓀴very painful experience
 I once received
 of a kitchen-maid in hysterics.
767𓀴People used to accept
 life and make
 their appeal to the intellect; now
768𓀴they spend their time screaming
 at natural laws and living for the
769𓀴emotions. It is a
 mysterious modern
 compulsion which used to be called
770𓀴selfishness. When I hear you begin
 to talk of your temperament, my dear
771𓀴Edith, I shall cease to ask
 you to run my errands.”
772𓀴
773𓀴Edith stroked her aunt’s hand
 and smiled at her; but she was
774𓀴preoccupied; she was
 expecting Horace.
775𓀴
776𓀴“Sometimes, Edith,
 you disappoint me.
 I have an impression that all the
777𓀴wisdom of all the ages, including
 my own, would have less effect upon
778𓀴your intelligence
 than the sound (I
 suppose they have no creak) of an
779𓀴ordinary pair of
 Bond Street boots.”
780𓀴
781𓀴“Well, he is rather
 late,” said Edith.
782𓀴
783𓀴“He is talking with his sister,”
 suggested Lady Walton. “I must
784𓀴congratulate you,
 my dear, in having
 chosen a husband who has complete
785𓀴ignorance of women.
 It is a very valuable
 attribute nowadays, when women
786𓀴have no restraint
 and men no manners.
 Horace is doubtless explaining to
787𓀴Miss Lestrange what an
 excellent arrangement
 his marriage will be for
788𓀴everybody concerned;
 and Miss Lestrange
 is turning his attention to
789𓀴awkward details. I
 hope you are prepared
 for complications, Edith; the
790𓀴maternal instinct of maiden aunts
 is a very fierce thing to combat. Do
791𓀴you realize that she may
 refuse to let the boy go?”
792𓀴
793𓀴The girl moved restlessly.
794𓀴
795𓀴“Oh, she can’t!” she
 murmured. “After
 all, Horace is very strong; he’s
796𓀴not a weak man, auntie.”
797𓀴
798𓀴“There is nothing so vulnerable as
 some kinds of strength,” said Lady
799𓀴Walton, with a sigh,
 “or so invulnerable
 as some kinds of weakness. What
800𓀴is tyranny but weakness playing
 on generosity, and how long do you
801𓀴suppose it can last? It can last
 as long as the generosity.”
802𓀴
803𓀴Edith shivered a little.
804𓀴
805𓀴“But he’ll think of me,” she said.
 “He knows how I want his child.”
806𓀴
807𓀴“He’ll think of you,”
 said her aunt
 very slowly; “yes, he’ll think of
808𓀴you, Edith; but thought
 doesn’t compel--there
 is only one compulsion.”
809𓀴
810𓀴It was surgery for
 the sake of healing,
 but the knife struck deep.
811𓀴
812𓀴Lady Walton sat quite
 still; she did
 not attempt to touch or soothe the
813𓀴girl; she did not

814𓀴

816𓀴have forbidden this

817𓀴what you are marrying for--and you

818𓀴are not fool enough to spend your

819𓀴the moon is an injurious

820𓀴thing one gets.”

826𓀴say--but Horace could have given

827𓀴already. And--and what is so much

828𓀴back the moon’s equivalent.

829𓀴and he can’t take it!”
830𓀴

832𓀴
833𓀴Edith hesitated a moment. Then she

835𓀴“I think I hear the taxi which is

836𓀴boots,” said her aunt, “and if you

837𓀴bed. It is a quarter

842𓀴

1002𓀴“At this moment he

1565𓀴“I am glad it seemed natural,”
 said Edith quietly.
1566𓀴
1567𓀴In most married
 lives there is one
 who understands, and one who is
1568𓀴content to be understood.
 Edith read
 her husband’s mind as if it were a
1569𓀴well-known book. She
 knew his motives,
 his honest scruples, his studious
1570𓀴chivalry, his quiet
 reticence. She
 knew when he suffered because the
1571𓀴marks of it were
 on her own heart;
 she knew when he was contented,
1572𓀴puzzled, worried, or pleased; and
 he knew nothing whatever about her,
1573𓀴except that she was always sweet
 to him, and only occasionally (as we
1574𓀴are told all women
 are) unreasonable.
 That he was sitting opposite a
1575𓀴woman now whose heart
 was very nearly
 broken, who had fed in secret on
1576𓀴sharp misery and
 long, ineffectual
 pain, had never even dimly touched
1577𓀴his imagination.
1578𓀴
1579𓀴Lady Walton had
 supposed that Edith
 might have a difficult time, but
1580𓀴that the quiet routine of married
 life would soon stifle the unnatural
1581𓀴hunger of her heart.
 Nothing had been
 stifled except expression. Edith
1582𓀴had thrust her own
 pain out of sight
 with strong hands; she had shut
1583𓀴love and anguish
 out of her eyes--she
 had schooled her lips not to
1584𓀴quiver and held her
 voice steady against
 the invasions of emotion; she
1585𓀴had taken stones for
 bread, and received
 them with pleased acquiescence,
1586𓀴as if, after all, her preference
 had always been for stones.
1587𓀴
1588𓀴But when it came
 to Horace’s pain,
 to the unstilled longing of his
1589𓀴heart, which she
 could have stilled;
 to the silent, patient endurance,
1590𓀴which she could have stirred into
 something resembling passionate joy;
1591𓀴then her life rose
 up against her,
 and the bitter waters of intense and
1592𓀴heavy anguish passed
 over her soul.
 Ah, the sickening pressure of those
1593𓀴hours! While he lay
 beside her sleeping
 quietly, she clenched her hands,
1594𓀴and quivered with
 the sobs she dared
 not free. And then with haggard
1595𓀴eyes she watched
 the slow day dawn
 over London, the day which would
1596𓀴be--as all other days to her--the
 resetting of her life to sober pain.
1597𓀴He thought her a sensible,
 level-headed,
 unemotional woman, and she was
1598𓀴a creature born
 of flame and tears!
1599𓀴
1600𓀴The hand that held
 the fire-screen
 shook a little.
1601𓀴
1602𓀴“You mustn’t let
 yourself worry about
 it,” said Horace kindly, “or I
1603𓀴shall be sorry I’ve
 told you. Honestly,
 I don’t think it will become a
1604𓀴serious matter.
 There’s the evening
 post. I rather imagine that
1605𓀴extremely fancy envelope
 may be from
 the lady herself. My correspondents
1606𓀴are usually less stimulating
 in their notepaper.”
1607𓀴
1608𓀴He read it, frowned in a puzzled
 way, and tossed it over to Edith. It
1609𓀴ran:
1610𓀴
1611𓀴    MY DEAR SIR,--Thank you for
 your simple, explicit statement of
1612𓀴    the case. You gave me a great
 deal of amusement, and no pain. I
1613𓀴    wish your son had copied your
 style. I daresay he has told you
1614𓀴    that he meant to marry me;
 but though an imaginative child, I
1615𓀴    don’t suppose
 he can ever have
 said to you that I meant to marry
1616𓀴    him. I don’t make promises,
 and unless any unforeseen
1617𓀴    circumstance should arise,
 I am not likely to undertake the
1618𓀴    experiment. As to my moral
 influence (which I notice you have
1619𓀴    done me the honor
 not to mention),
 I have always made it my
1620𓀴    invariable rule to leave boys
 alone. I laugh at them, but I do
1621𓀴    not hurt them.
1622𓀴
1623𓀴                             
                 Yours sincerely,
1624𓀴                                
                  ANASTASIA FALAISE.
1625𓀴
1626𓀴“Well, what do you make of that,
 my dear?” said Horace Lestrange,
1627𓀴feeling after his matches. “Seems
 to let us out, doesn’t it?”
1628𓀴
1629𓀴Edith let the paper fall into
 her lap and looked into the fire.
1630𓀴
1631𓀴“Poor woman!” she
 said very gently.
 “Do you know, Horace, she reminds me
1632𓀴just a little of
 Helen of Troy. Helen
 used to talk like that, as if the
1633𓀴soul had been eaten
 out of her words.
 I think a woman must be very
1634𓀴unhappy to write like that.”
1635𓀴
1636𓀴“It is time you
 went to bed, Edith;
 you look tired,” said her husband.
1637𓀴“Just throw that letter
 on the fire, will you?”
1638𓀴
1639𓀴She threw the letter on to
 the fire and watched it burn.
1640𓀴
1641𓀴“Poor woman!” she whispered
 to herself again. “Poor woman!”
1642𓀴
1643𓀴Horace got up and opened the door
 for her. He was very much relieved,
1644𓀴but he felt a pang of compunction
 at the same time. He had been a fool
1645𓀴to tell Edith; it seemed to upset
 her; the facts of life--love’s
1646𓀴tragedies--ought to be kept from
 good women. Then he went back to his
1647𓀴paper.
1648𓀴
1649𓀴
1650𓀴                
                  VII
1651𓀴
1652𓀴Like many people who believe in
 an over-ruling Providence, Miss
1653𓀴Lestrange never left anything to
 it. On the contrary, when her plans
1654𓀴succeeded, she remarked
 triumphantly
 that it was the will of heaven; and
1655𓀴when they failed,
 she said nothing
 about it, and tried again. It is
1656𓀴usually supposed that plans which
 play the part of Providence fail very
1657𓀴easily, but this
 is not really so;
 it is only the result of the plan
1658𓀴that fails--carefully
 combined arrangements
 made with due knowledge of
1659𓀴the forces of life
 seldom fail. What
 fails is what we expected to win
1660𓀴from such combinations.
 You plant,
 water, and gain your increase, and
1661𓀴what you thought were the golden
 apples of the Hesperides taste like
1662𓀴dust.
1663𓀴
1664𓀴This is what happened
 to Miss Lestrange.
 She gave a whole-hearted
1665𓀴devotion to Leslie;
 she kept him away
 from what she honestly believed to
1666𓀴be adverse influences; she cared
 for the delicate little boy until he
1667𓀴became as strong as the average
 youth; she made her home his home;
1668𓀴people always referred
 to him as “your dear boy.”
1669𓀴
1670𓀴This was the palace
 of her dreams,
 but the monarch had abdicated, and
1671𓀴the palace without its King
 is a Court in mourning.
1672𓀴
1673𓀴Leslie was vaguely
 dissatisfied; he
 had worshiped his Aunt Etta with an
1674𓀴ignorant devotion
 all his life; he
 had given her the love of a child and
1675𓀴the warm-hearted
 loyalty of a boy.
 Now he was grown up. He was nineteen,
1676𓀴and he would probably
 never be quite
 as old again--in any case, he would
1677𓀴never feel such
 unbroken confidence
 in his own judgment--and what did
1678𓀴his aunt appear? A small,
 faded, old-fashioned
 woman, who said “No” to
1679𓀴his wishes.
1680𓀴
1681𓀴There is a time
 in every boy’s life
 when he looks very narrowly at his
1682𓀴own parents; very often they are
 the barriers at the gates of his
1683𓀴imaginary Paradise,
 and he regards
 them as barriers; but if there is
1684𓀴solid stuff in the youth, the tie
 is strong enough to hold. His parents
1685𓀴are, of course, wrong,
 their opinions
 are worthless, their ideas are
1686𓀴effete and purely
 mirth-inspiring.
 But they are his parents. They are
1687𓀴people who love
 him with a strange
 love; they are ignorant people, but
1688𓀴he forgives them,
 and one day discovers
 that he himself belongs to this
1689𓀴inferior branch
 of humanity, and is
 giving his life up for his sons, who
1690𓀴regard him in his turn with
 affectionate depreciation.
1691𓀴
1692𓀴Leslie loved his
 father with a deep
 natural love, which time turned into
1693𓀴an irritated need. He had come to
 the conclusion that women were all
1694𓀴very well, but that
 feminine relations
 were a jealous bore, and
1695𓀴that--you must see life.
1696𓀴
1697𓀴So he saw life. Saw it immaturely
 and unwisely--or rather he may have
1698𓀴been said not to see it, but with
 the rush of youth’s music in his ears
1699𓀴he ran blindfold, and Life mocked
 him to her heart’s content, and gave
1700𓀴him pebbles for
 diamonds and dross
 for gold, till she blunted alike his
1701𓀴discrimination and his growth.
1702𓀴
1703𓀴Miss Lestrange stood by watching
 him with incompetent agony. She had
1704𓀴seen these things happen before
 to other people’s boys, and she had
1705𓀴always known why.
 The mothers were
 silly, the boys were unlicked
1706𓀴cubs--they had been spoilt from
 the first. Now she was not quite so
1707𓀴sure. Perhaps such
 things happened
 just out of misfortune, unhappiness,
1708𓀴blunders that must
 come, accidents
 of the type which are said to haunt
1709𓀴families who live according
 to the best regulations.
1710𓀴
1711𓀴Lines came into Miss
 Lestrange’s placid
 face, she lost sleep, appetite,
1712𓀴and repose. She woke
 with vague terrors,
 she was haunted by impotent
1713𓀴fears. There was
 nothing to be done,
 and she hadn’t the strength to do
1714𓀴nothing. Finally, the whole story
 focused on one notorious lady of
1715𓀴musical comedy.
 The youth of London
 gave her desperate homage and
1716𓀴adoration; she was
 old enough to be
 their mother; but they did not keep
1717𓀴these gifts for
 their mothers, they
 gave them to Anastasia Falaise, and
1718𓀴she accepted them
 with easy laughter.
1719𓀴
1720𓀴Miss Lestrange wrote
 to her brother,
 and her brother replied heartlessly
1721𓀴that it was “all right.”
1722𓀴
1723𓀴Leslie went on worshipping
 at this
 popular shrine; he was continually
1724𓀴absent from Mallows;
 if he was present
 he was silent when he wasn’t
1725𓀴irritable. He never
 mentioned the lady’s
 name, but he wrote to her every
1726𓀴day; she wrote to him sometimes,
 and vague ideas, resisted by common
1727𓀴sense, prompted Miss
 Lestrange to tamper
 with their correspondence. This
1728𓀴correspondence flourished
 even more
 conspicuously after the ineffective
1729𓀴efforts of Leslie’s
 father. It was
 evident to Miss Lestrange that there
1730𓀴was no help to be
 met in that quarter,
 so she attacked the citadel
1731𓀴itself. With nervous
 incoherence she
 implored Leslie to come abroad, to
1732𓀴give up this absurd
 infatuation. Leslie
 raised youth’s deadly standard
1733𓀴of silence. He blocked her
 utterance with a gloomy stare.
1734𓀴
1735𓀴Finally, he observed
 that he did not
 know what she meant, and left the
1736𓀴room. Young men and even old ones
 are to be congratulated on this gift
1737𓀴of absence; it is
 a very effective
 weapon. Leslie did not return for
1738𓀴some time; when he
 did, Miss Lestrange
 said nothing further on the
1739𓀴subject, for she had
 stayed in the room.
1740𓀴
1741𓀴Finally, goaded
 to desperation, she
 committed an unprecedented error.
1742𓀴She may, indeed,
 have been described
 as completely losing her head. She
1743𓀴went to call one
 day by appointment
 on Anastasia Falaise.
1744𓀴
1745𓀴Anastasia was staying in a famous
 London hotel. She had a charming
1746𓀴sitting-room; it
 was littered with
 presentations, and she sat shaded by
1747𓀴pink blinds with easy indolence
 in a large armchair.
1748𓀴
1749𓀴Miss Lestrange’s first impression
 may be given as it flashed into her
1750𓀴mind, “No woman of that type has
 any right to be so beautiful.”
1751𓀴Anastasia showed
 neither youth nor
 years in her face; she might as
1752𓀴easily have been thirty as fifty;
 she had no lines about her eyes and
1753𓀴mouth or marring her
 low Greek forehead.
 Her wide-set dark eyes looked
1754𓀴like some perennial
 mysterious spring
 of life. Her face and neck and
1755𓀴hands were the color
 of warm ivory;
 her black hair was natural, but as
1756𓀴nobody believed it,
 she would sometimes--to
 confirm it--let coil after
1757𓀴coil fall to her knees.
 She had beauty
 as some men have genius, and she
1758𓀴used it with more shrewdness and
 common sense than this other gift is
1759𓀴often used. She had no particular
 wish to please Miss Lestrange, so she
1760𓀴simply stared at her.
1761𓀴
1762𓀴Miss Lestrange was
 vaguely uncomfortable;
 she felt that she was with an
1763𓀴extraordinary person,
 and that she
 had lowered herself to the same level
1764𓀴by doing an extraordinary
 thing. This
 was the kind of woman she knew how
1765𓀴to snub; she did not know
 how to appeal to her.
1766𓀴
1767𓀴“I think you know
 my nephew, Leslie
 Lestrange,” she began, blushing a
1768𓀴little at her companion’s
 insolent, inanimate beauty.
1769𓀴
1770𓀴“There are half-a-dozen
 photographs
 of him, and the contents of several
1771𓀴jewelers’ shops,
 I should fancy, just
 behind you,” observed Anastasia.
1772𓀴“Have you come to
 retrieve them? I
 told him that unless his people were
1773𓀴whole-sale jewelers he had better
 try a less expensive amusement.”
1774𓀴
1775𓀴“His family is one of the oldest
 in England,” said Miss Lestrange
1776𓀴impressively.
1777𓀴
1778𓀴“Well, he’s young
 enough,” observed
 the imperturbable beauty. She had a
1779𓀴slight American intonation which
 Miss Lestrange found strangely
1780𓀴aggravating; it
 annoyed her almost
 beyond the power of speech.
1781𓀴
1782𓀴“I have always taken
 the greatest interest
 in my nephew’s concerns,” she
1783𓀴continued. “I have brought him up
 from his babyhood. I stand to him in
1784𓀴the place of his parents.”
1785𓀴
1786𓀴“And yet I had a
 very sensible letter
 from his father the other day,”
1787𓀴interrupted Anastasia,
 and she laughed

1788𓀴pleasure (which Miss Lestrange
 promptly mistook for vulgar
1789𓀴impertinence). “I
 think it is the most
 sensible letter I ever had, and I
1790𓀴answered it. I guess he hasn’t
 sent you here, has he?”
1791𓀴
1792𓀴“My brother married regrettably
 a second time,” said Miss Lestrange
1793𓀴coldly, “a woman of
 no family connections,
 singularly unsuited to bring
1794𓀴up a delicate and
 sensitive child;
 even her husband has never pressed
1795𓀴the point.”
1796𓀴
1797𓀴“You don’t say,”
 observed Anastasia,
 narrowly regarding her exquisite
1798𓀴fingers. “Poor disconnected lady,
 I feel quite sorry for her!”
1799𓀴
1800𓀴“On the contrary,” replied
 Miss Lestrange,
 “she has, I think, been very
1801𓀴fortunate; a marriage of that
 kind for a girl in Edith Walton’s
1802𓀴position, and at her age--she was
 thirty at the time--does not happen
1803𓀴every day over here.”
1804𓀴
1805𓀴Anastasia suddenly
 woke up for the
 first time; she opened her great eyes
1806𓀴wide and looked
 at Miss Lestrange.
 It was a look so vital, so amazingly
1807𓀴keen, staring out of

1808𓀴Lestrange jumped.
1809𓀴
1810𓀴Then Anastasia sank back into her
 usual attitude of inspired indolence.
1811𓀴
1812𓀴“What did you say her name was?”
 she asked languidly. “Haddlestone? I
1813𓀴knew some people called
 that once--way out West.”
1814𓀴
1815𓀴“No, Walton,” repeated
 Miss Lestrange
 distinctly; “but it really hardly
1816𓀴matters what her
 name is, I think,
 to the subject under discussion.”
1817𓀴
1818𓀴“Were you discussing
 anything?” Anastasia
 asked calmly. “I wasn’t. I am
1819𓀴merely wasting my
 time. However, I
 won’t waste any more of it. What do
1820𓀴you want?” and her voice suddenly
 turned brisk and business-like.
1821𓀴
1822𓀴“I want you,” said Miss Lestrange
 with a sudden quiver of pathetic
1823𓀴middle-aged passion,
 “to let my boy
 go. You are a beautiful woman; what
1824𓀴does one boy more or less matter
 to you--a practically penniless boy,
1825𓀴too? Send him about his business,
 like a--like a kind-hearted woman.”
1826𓀴
1827𓀴“How do you know
 I am a kind-hearted
 woman?” Anastasia asked curiously.
1828𓀴
1829𓀴“Because,” said Miss
 Lestrange, rising
 to her feet, “you have all the
1830𓀴advantages on your side; you
 can easily afford to be.”
1831𓀴
1832𓀴“Well, I do call
 that cute!” drawled
 Anastasia. “That’s the best thing
1833𓀴you’ve said yet,
 only it’s not true.
 However, we needn’t go into that.
1834𓀴Now, Miss Lestrange, you’ve made
 a great mistake; if you had left the
1835𓀴matter in your brother’s

 never have heard of it again--that
1836𓀴is to say, you wouldn’t seriously

1837𓀴other, you’ve put
 an idea into my head;
 well, that was a mistake. I have
1838𓀴very few ideas, and
 I always act upon
 them. I’m going to act right now;
1839𓀴but I don’t want an

1840𓀴
1841𓀴Anastasia rose too.
 She was head and
 shoulders above her companion, and
1842𓀴Miss Lestrange drew a long breath

1843𓀴figure. This was a woman to wreck

1844𓀴her head about a boy--a boy like

1845𓀴even know, whose disabilities she
 must, of course, see? It was all very
1846𓀴odd. The two women looked

1847𓀴
1848𓀴“I can’t understand you,” said
 Miss Lestrange at last, a little
1849𓀴helplessly, “and I don’t see that
 I can offer you anything you want in
1850𓀴exchange for what I ask.”
1851𓀴
1852𓀴“You can’t,” said
 Anastasia; “nobody
 can offer me what I want except
1853𓀴chocolates. Fortunately,
 I’m still
 very fond of chocolates. Well,
1854𓀴good-by, Miss Lestrange;
 I’m sorry
 I can’t oblige you, but I’ve got to
1855𓀴be amused, and I am going to

1856𓀴
1857𓀴“Oh, amuse yourself

1858𓀴holding out her hand in farewell,
 “but don’t marry him!”
1859𓀴
1860𓀴“I guess you’re going

 observed Anastasia, as her
1861𓀴companion, reaching

1862𓀴you’re going to feel
 disconnected, too!”
1863𓀴
1864𓀴Miss Lestrange didn’t
 know what her
 hostess meant, but she had said all
1865𓀴she had to say and

 do. There was nothing more to act
1866𓀴upon, and she knew

1867𓀴
1868𓀴Suddenly Miss Lestrange
 felt old and

1869𓀴accompanied her--a

1870𓀴position--which made her observe

1871𓀴right to a front seat

1872𓀴was, after all, only
 one of the crowd,
 liable to be pushed and jogged by
1873𓀴elbows, even liable to be thrust

1874𓀴still in the finely
 upholstered lounge

1875𓀴came up and asked her if
 he could bring her anything.
1876𓀴
1877𓀴“Yes,” said Miss
 Lestrange, sitting
 down at one of the many little
1878𓀴tables scattered about. “You may
 bring me a cup of tea. Perhaps,” she
1879𓀴said to herself, “that was what
 I wanted. I have missed my tea.”
1880𓀴
1881𓀴
1882𓀴                 
                 VIII
1883𓀴
1884𓀴Edith hardly turned
 her head to say
 “Come in!” to the timid knock at her
1885𓀴door. She was sitting
 at her desk,
 doing accounts, and puzzled as usual
1886𓀴by her immaculate predecessor’s
 example--an
 example which, “as the most
1887𓀴sensible of women,”
 she tried hard
 to follow, but she was frequently
1888𓀴overcome by the invincible malice
 of pounds, shillings, and pence.
1889𓀴
1890𓀴The pause, however,
 that followed arrested
 her attention, and she turned
1891𓀴to meet the eyes
 of her step-son with
 a thrill of astonishment. He had
1892𓀴never before voluntarily
 entered her
 private boudoir, and there was an
1893𓀴air about his whole person which
 betokened the unusual, though he
1894𓀴suppressed what he
 could only consider
 a weakness as well as he could.
1895𓀴
1896𓀴Edith saw in a moment
 that she must
 suppress it too. “I’m so glad you
1897𓀴have come,” she
 said; “now you can
 do this horrid sum for me. I am
1898𓀴trying to balance
 my accounts, and
 though I can see quite plainly what
1899𓀴I’ve spent and what
 I had to spend,
 they obstinately refuse to have
1900𓀴anything to do with each other.”
1901𓀴
1902𓀴Leslie looked over her shoulder;
 he was pleased to point out her
1903𓀴mistake--it was a
 very obvious one--and
 it at once put him at his ease.
1904𓀴He felt there could
 be nothing very
 formidable in a woman who could make
1905𓀴such a silly mistake
 in quite a simple sum.
1906𓀴
1907𓀴He sat down beside
 her, smiling and
 looking so utterly unlike the glum,
1908𓀴discontented youth

1909𓀴conceal her astonishment.
1910𓀴
1911𓀴“I’ve got an awful
 lot to say to you,”
 he volunteered at last. “What a
1912𓀴jolly little room
 you have here--just
 the kind of things I like!”
1913𓀴
1914𓀴“Well, you must come and like
 them a little oftener,” said his
1915𓀴step-mother with

1916𓀴
1917𓀴He glanced at her uneasily.
1918𓀴
1919𓀴“I expect I must
 seem an awful ass
 to you,” he remarked with sudden
1920𓀴candor.
1921𓀴
1922𓀴Edith shook her head.
1923𓀴
1924𓀴“Dear no,” she said,
 “nor am I a very
 terrible person either, when you
1925𓀴come to know me!”
1926𓀴
1927𓀴“Oh, you,” said the boy, flushing
 scarlet--“you’re ripping! I can’t
1928𓀴think why I’ve never
 noticed it before.”
1929𓀴
1930𓀴Edith concealed a
 smile at this belated
 tribute; she wondered what he
1931𓀴was going to notice next.
1932𓀴
1933𓀴“Would you mind,” he
 began anxiously--“are
 you quite sure you wouldn’t
1934𓀴mind, if I came
 here regularly--in
 between terms at Oxford, I
1935𓀴mean--instead of
 going to Mallows?”
1936𓀴
1937𓀴Edith gasped. Then she said
 very gently and gravely:
1938𓀴
1939𓀴“My dear Leslie,
 this is your home.”
1940𓀴
1941𓀴He got up and walked
 about; she hadn’t
 used her advantage over him; she
1942𓀴hadn’t even made him look a fool.
 He was almost willing to acknowledge
1943𓀴that he was one.
1944𓀴
1945𓀴“I think I’d like
 to tell you all about

1946𓀴sha’n’t bore you?”
1947𓀴
1948𓀴“No, you won’t bore me,”
 said his companion.
1949𓀴
1950𓀴“I daresay you know--I
 daresay you

1951𓀴Anastasia Falaise? Of course, you
 don’t know what she’s like; people
1952𓀴talk such confounded
 rot about her,
 especially women. You should hear
1953𓀴Aunt Etta. They say she’s old; of
 course, it’s all jealousy. She may be
1954𓀴twenty-five--that’s
 older than me,

1955𓀴(his nineteenth
 birthday had taken

1956𓀴what’s five years?”
1957𓀴
1958𓀴His step-mother
 was not prepared to
 say off-hand what five years were;
1959𓀴they might be such different

1960𓀴sympathetically

1961𓀴
1962𓀴“People talk such beastly stuff

1963𓀴fiercely, “and not

1964𓀴perfect. Why, Edith--Cleopatra,

1965𓀴Troy--they couldn’t

1966𓀴the poets are all really idiots;

1967𓀴
1968𓀴Edith looked as if

1969𓀴thought that the poets
 had under-estimated
 Horace, but that was very
1970𓀴probably because they were,
 generally speaking, men.
1971𓀴
1972𓀴“Do you know, I can’t believe in

1973𓀴might have married princes, and

1974𓀴
1975𓀴Edith’s eyes filled with sudden

1976𓀴young, exquisitely

1977𓀴for the first time she was seeing

1978𓀴of life!
1979𓀴
1980𓀴“I can quite believe it,
 Leslie,” she said gently.
1981𓀴
1982𓀴“And she’s promised to marry me,”
 he exclaimed exultantly, “in three
1983𓀴years’ time.”
1984𓀴
1985𓀴His step-mother jumped.
 This was not

1986𓀴It came with a sudden
 shock. Horace
 had said the woman was old enough to
1987𓀴be the boy’s mother, and Horace
 was certain to be right.
1988𓀴
1989𓀴“Oh, Leslie!” she

1990𓀴distressed. “Oh, Leslie!”
1991𓀴

1993𓀴and, fancy, Aunt
 Etta--well--I can

1994𓀴between us, and actually went and

1995𓀴life she’s tried to keep me away

1996𓀴Anastasia! I can’t

 Leslie, “and I shouldn’t think you
1997𓀴would.”
1998𓀴
1999𓀴He took one of Edith’s

2000𓀴
2001𓀴“Oh, my dear boy,” she whispered,
 “you don’t know, you don’t understand
2002𓀴how she loved you!
 You see you did

2003𓀴little one that didn’t

2004𓀴may matter terribly

 Etta. Ah, Leslie, she’s given up her
2005𓀴life for you--she

2006𓀴Try to forgive her!”
2007𓀴
2008𓀴“I’d have forgiven

2009𓀴on the sly. Father

2010𓀴told me he was going to--he

2011𓀴
2012𓀴The boy choked suddenly.
2013𓀴
2014𓀴“Do you know,” he

2015𓀴awfully kind to me.”
2016𓀴

2018𓀴years ago through happy tears as

2019𓀴son.
2020𓀴
2021𓀴“Oh, Leslie, Leslie!”
 she murmured.
2022𓀴
2023𓀴He was not a demonstrative

 so he kicked at a footstool, and
2024𓀴gave rather a foolish laugh.
2025𓀴
2026𓀴“Well, it’ll all

 he said. “Anastasia is most awfully
2027𓀴keen on my being

 She slanged me fearfully for not
2028𓀴living at home--pitched

2029𓀴
2030𓀴“Did she?” said
 Edith thoughtfully.
 “I wonder why?”
2031𓀴
2032𓀴“Oh, she’s so awfully
 clever and generous,
 you know.” The boy went on:
2033𓀴“She said she was sure

2034𓀴the least I could do was

2035𓀴
2036𓀴Edith suddenly rose

2037𓀴trembled, and there was a

2038𓀴
2039𓀴“Have you,” she asked, “a picture

2040𓀴
2041𓀴The boy laughed shamefacedly.

2044𓀴tossed it with a pretence

2051𓀴

2053𓀴glad you like it!”
2054𓀴

2056𓀴the boy took it from

2060𓀴everything she ought

2062𓀴
2063𓀴“I am forty,” said

2064𓀴
2065𓀴Leslie looked up compassionately.
2066𓀴
2067𓀴“Well,” he said

2068𓀴Edith.”
2069𓀴
2070𓀴“No, I’m not really old yet,”

2071𓀴
2072𓀴Helen of Troy was forty-two.
2073𓀴
2074𓀴A long silence followed.
 The boy began

2075𓀴and choose some flowers

2076𓀴

2082𓀴
2083𓀴He kissed her and

2084𓀴
2085𓀴
2086𓀴                
                   IX
2087𓀴
2088𓀴Anastasia was dressed
 to go out in
 the Park. It was an exquisite day of
2089𓀴early spring. Winter had lingered
 longer than usual and the green world
2090𓀴had been for some time pining and
 cheerless, an unfilled canvas waiting
2091𓀴for its artist--the sun. The park
 was a shimmering sea of verdant
2092𓀴new-born foliage and young spring
 flowers. Crocuses and daffodils and
2093𓀴hyacinths made summer
 in the midst of London.
2094𓀴
2095𓀴Everybody who was
 anybody wandered
 or drove or motored in its precincts,
2096𓀴or sat on the green chairs under
 the trees and looked at each other’s
2097𓀴clothes, and speculated
 why So-and-so
 was--or was not--with somebody
2098𓀴else; and somehow or other spring
 struck a note of freshness into even
2099𓀴the stalest speculations, and did
 its best to prick the heart towards
2100𓀴beauty and delight.
2101𓀴
2102𓀴Anastasia was dressed to join the
 distinguished throng. It was her
2103𓀴world, and she knew
 that she would
 be followed by whispers, criticisms,
2104𓀴and speculation,
 even as she would
 be joined by groups of privileged
2105𓀴young men, very good-looking,
 well-dressed,
 ardent, and most terribly
2106𓀴silly--and she knew
 that none of this
 would amuse her very much, and yet
2107𓀴that if it failed
 her, and when it
 failed her, there would be nothing
2108𓀴else. She was dressed
 in white and
 orange, and as she looked at the
2109𓀴superb curves of
 her figure, at the
 classical white face and wide dark
2110𓀴eyes, at the huge coils
 of her magnificent
 black hair, she smiled a
2111𓀴little. “Keats to-day,” she said
 to herself, “ye ardent marigolds!”
2112𓀴
2113𓀴Then she turned round and
 faced Edith Lestrange.
2114𓀴
2115𓀴“I came up unannounced,”
 said Edith.
 “I said you expected me. I don’t
2116𓀴know whether you did. Oh,
 Helen--Helen; it’s you!”
2117𓀴
2118𓀴Helen of Troy stood
 quite still, her

2119𓀴she stood there
 a change came over
 her face; it was the same face, and
2120𓀴yet the years came out in it--the
 suppressed, ignored, and baffled
2121𓀴years; she could no

2122𓀴twenty-five.
2123𓀴
2124𓀴Edith came forward,
 her hands outstretched.
2125𓀴
2126𓀴“Oh, Helen,” she
 said with a quiver
 in her voice, “am I so old you don’t
2127𓀴remember me--twenty years ago?”
2128𓀴
2129𓀴“Don’t!” said Helen of Troy.
2130𓀴
2131𓀴She moistened her
 lips and put her
 hands up to her throat, then suddenly
2132𓀴she began to laugh at first, just
 her old velvety laugh of music, and
2133𓀴then suddenly distorted,
 bitter laughter--terrible

2134𓀴harmony run mad.
2135𓀴
2136𓀴“Oh, I remember you!”
 she cried between

2137𓀴remember you all right, Edith.”
2138𓀴
2139𓀴Edith came forward
 quietly; her face
 was very white and her eyes looked
2140𓀴drawn and tired, but she drew the
 orange and white figure shaking with
2141𓀴its bitter laughter to the

2142𓀴
2143𓀴“I know--I know,” she whispered
 gently; “don’t laugh so, Helen.”
2144𓀴
2145𓀴“It’s all so funny,”
 laughed Helen
 of Troy, “so ghastly, ridiculously,
2146𓀴agonizingly funny,
 and he might be
 your own son or mine, my dear--only
2147𓀴we haven’t any!”
2148𓀴
2149𓀴“We haven’t any,” repeated Edith.
 “Now, Helen, give me your hand. See,
2150𓀴it’s very cold--and
 now your other

2151𓀴difference--nothing has

 You should have come to see
2152𓀴me. When did you first know

2153𓀴
2154𓀴Helen had found her self-control

2155𓀴sofa-cushion with yielded hands

2156𓀴companion.
2157𓀴
2158𓀴“Oh, not till Miss
 Lestrange came.

2159𓀴him another thought,

2160𓀴then she let out suddenly

2161𓀴

2163𓀴

2166𓀴

2169𓀴

 you’re forty-two,” she said at
2333𓀴length.
2334𓀴
2335𓀴Helen threw back her

2336𓀴
2337𓀴“It isn’t twenty years ago,” she
 murmured. “It’s ten minutes. Now,
2338𓀴Edith, if you’ll take
 my advice you’ll
 not decide this yourself. You
2339𓀴seem to have overlooked for the

2340𓀴existence. Does he
 know what happened
 twenty minutes--years ago--I
2341𓀴mean?”
2342𓀴
2343𓀴“Oh, yes,” said Edith;
 “I told him.”
2344𓀴
2345𓀴“Very well, then,”
 said Helen of Troy.
 “I happen to know that Leslie is
2346𓀴dining out to-night.
 I will, therefore,

2347𓀴you. Do you trust me, Edith?”

2350𓀴much harm with a mere meal,

2352𓀴Helen stooped towards

2353𓀴
2354𓀴“After all,” she said, “I’ve had

2355𓀴this.”

2359𓀴
2360𓀴Horace was slightly
 surprised on coming

2361𓀴in his wife’s sitting-room

 such widely-spread picture-postcard
2362𓀴fame. He had already

2363𓀴she had made famous,

2364𓀴

2366𓀴
2367𓀴For a moment he was baffled by

2368𓀴sacrifice of the

2369𓀴came back to him. He held

2370𓀴
2371𓀴“I am most happy to meet Helen
 of Troy,” he said, smiling.
2372𓀴
2373𓀴There was no one

2374𓀴picture of delicate

2375𓀴an excellent host,

2376𓀴She drew out their best with

2377𓀴participating so much as forming

2378𓀴pleasant and easy to speak.
2379𓀴
2380𓀴“I always could say

2384𓀴
2385𓀴“We’re so simple

 “We are like an old tune to a
2386𓀴practised singer; we

2388𓀴“Oh, you’re not dull,”

2389𓀴simple as all this,

2390𓀴we’re clever, we’re

2391𓀴world, but we’re not simple.”
2392𓀴
2393𓀴“You can’t expect a young nation

2394𓀴said Horace. “English people have

2395𓀴time. They stand on

 Now all the Americans I ever met
2396𓀴wanted to be individual,

2397𓀴When they were not, it was rather

2398𓀴don’t want, as a rule, to be like

2399𓀴it for us; but I

 heart of hearts we don’t think it
2400𓀴very solid. There’s something in

2401𓀴knowing that among your own class

2402𓀴purpose, the same

2403𓀴as well, because

2404𓀴much that can be

2406𓀴Helen gave him a good

2407𓀴he wasn’t in the

2408𓀴had imagined him

2410𓀴not see everything,

2411𓀴began in his never having looked

2412𓀴proposed to her,

2413𓀴were that she would go on doing

2414𓀴interfered.
2415𓀴
2416𓀴“What he wants,”

2417𓀴conversation, “is a shaking, and

2421𓀴

2423𓀴we stayed on the lakes;

2424𓀴like one of the little clouds we

2426𓀴know where it is?”
2427𓀴
2428𓀴Edith shook her head.
2429𓀴
2430𓀴“At the bottom of one of my old

2431𓀴treasures,” she said. “I could

2432𓀴

2434𓀴for granted that what she wanted

2435𓀴forthcoming.
2436𓀴
2437𓀴Edith laughed.
2438𓀴

2599𓀴
