                 ROSE
2617𓀴
2618𓀴
2619𓀴                  
             CHAPTER I
2620𓀴
2621𓀴The Pinsents never saw any reason
 why they shouldn’t be modern
2622𓀴without--as they expressed
 it--going too far.
2623𓀴
2624𓀴They didn’t believe
 in the sheltered-life
 system, but that was perhaps
2625𓀴because they rather
 under-estimated
 their own idea of what constituted a
2626𓀴shelter.
2627𓀴
2628𓀴There were certain
 risks, of course,
 in allowing your daughters to play
2629𓀴mixed hockey, smoke
 cigarettes and
 belong to a suffrage movement (they
2630𓀴could attend meetings,
 but weren’t
 to throw stones). Still, it was
2631𓀴strange how little
 harm these concessions
 to modernity had done the
2632𓀴Pinsent girls.
2633𓀴
2634𓀴Bernard Shaw rolled off them
 like water from a duck’s back.
2635𓀴
2636𓀴Somewhere between
 the ages of fourteen
 and seventeen Mrs. Pinsent
2637𓀴presented her daughters
 with an approximate
 definition of life. Agatha
2638𓀴yawned and Edith said, “Oh, dear!
 We knew all that ages ago.” For a
2639𓀴moment Mrs. Pinsent
 became agitated.
 Had they, in spite of the
2640𓀴healthiness of their
 surroundings,
 come in contact with evil influences?
2641𓀴But she was reassured when Agatha
 explained that they had picked it up
2642𓀴from rabbits.
2643𓀴
2644𓀴Rose, who was more sensitive and
 less observant, gave her mother more
2645𓀴trouble than the others,
 but she acquiesced
 at last, that God must know
2646𓀴best, though it all
 seemed rather funny.
 They were not to earn their own
2647𓀴livings because
 later on--(later on
 being the term in which the Pinsent
2648𓀴parents envisaged
 their retreat from
 this world) they would have plenty
2649𓀴of money; but they were expected
 to develop hobbies.
2650𓀴
2651𓀴The eldest girls
 developed splendid
 hobbies. Agatha, who was the
2652𓀴plainest of the three, became a
 lawn tennis champion with a really
2653𓀴smashing serve.
 Edith distinguished
 herself by writing a history of one
2654𓀴of our western counties.
 She rode all
 over it on a bicycle and stayed at
2655𓀴vicarages by herself.
 She earned a
 hundred pounds by this adventure and
2656𓀴had a particularly pleasant
 notice in the “Spectator.”
2657𓀴
2658𓀴Rose was rather slower
 at taking anything
 up. She had had pneumonia when
2659𓀴she was at school, and it had
 left her nominally but not at all
2660𓀴obstructively delicate.
2661𓀴
2662𓀴She played an excellent
 game of hockey
 and was her father’s favorite.
2663𓀴
2664𓀴It was really for
 Rose’s sake that
 they all decided to go to Rome.
2665𓀴
2666𓀴They thought Rose would
 almost certainly
 settle down to something after
2667𓀴that, and it would be good for
 Edith, who, now she had finished
2668𓀴Somersetshire, might
 like to begin on Rome.
2669𓀴
2670𓀴She could at any rate compare the
 different types of architecture. A
2671𓀴friend of hers,
 a Mr. Bunning, said
 there wasn’t any architecture in
2672𓀴Rome, but you could
 never be quite
 sure what Mr. Bunning meant. Edith
2673𓀴hadn’t been quite
 sure for several
 years--nor apparently had Mr.
2674𓀴Bunning, but perhaps their going
 to Rome might help him to find out.
2675𓀴Agatha was very
 good-natured about
 it; she said she thought Rome would
2676𓀴do as well as anywhere else.
2677𓀴
2678𓀴The Pinsents were
 a most accommodating
 family and though, of course,
2679𓀴they sometimes quarreled, it was
 all in a loud, direct, natural way,
2680𓀴which generally ended in chaff.
2681𓀴
2682𓀴They never quarreled
 with Rose as much
 as with each other because of her
2683𓀴having been rather
 delicate, still
 they chaffed her a good deal. She
2684𓀴wouldn’t have liked
 it if they hadn’t.
2685𓀴
2686𓀴They knew they weren’t like other
 families of their class and standing;
2687𓀴they prided themselves on talking
 to people in railway carriages and
2688𓀴even crossing the
 Channel. Of course,
 they were particularly good
2689𓀴sailors but even if they hadn’t
 been they would have been nice and
2690𓀴friendly and not at all
 stuck up about being sick.
2691𓀴
2692𓀴Agatha was thinking of marrying a
 Canadian who took most magnificent
2693𓀴back-handers, Edith
 was still wondering
 what Mr. Bunning meant, but Rose
2694𓀴was perfectly free.
2695𓀴
2696𓀴She’d had two proposals, but both
 of them had been from men she had
2697𓀴known all her life and liked most
 awfully--but not in that way. So that
2698𓀴she’d had, as Mrs. Pinsent put
 it to her husband, “quite a lot of
2699𓀴experience for twenty-one and
 none of the bother of it.”
2700𓀴
2701𓀴Mr. Pinsent growled and said that
 if Rose married the right kind of man
2702𓀴she never would have any bother.
2703𓀴
2704𓀴Mrs. Pinsent looked thoughtful;
 she didn’t want to think that Mr.
2705𓀴Pinsent was the
 wrong kind of man,
 it would have been dreadful after
2706𓀴being married to him
 for thirty years.
 Still, she couldn’t honestly have
2707𓀴said that she hadn’t
 had any bother with him.
2708𓀴
2709𓀴Probably Mr. Pinsent
 had forgotten
 it; men do not remember that kind of
2710𓀴thing in the same way.
2711𓀴
2712𓀴They chose a French hotel in Rome
 because they thought it would be more
2713𓀴Italian, and when
 they arrived there
 everything was just as foreign as
2714𓀴possible, which was
 what the Pinsents
 wanted--provided that they could
2715𓀴get enough hot water.
2716𓀴
2717𓀴The Hotel le Roy was even for
 Rome extraordinarily “black.” Its
2718𓀴_clientèle_ was
 composed of French
 priests, their sisters, ladies of
2719𓀴pronounced age and
 severity, one or
 two French families of prehistoric
2720𓀴claims, small means
 and a son at a
 seminary, and a few Dutch Catholics
2721𓀴who were, if anything, blacker
 than the French, but distinctly
2722𓀴pleasanter to the English. Black
 French Catholics do not like English
2723𓀴Protestants. The war
 may have softened
 this feeling, but this episode
2724𓀴took place a year
 before the war, when
 the _Entente Cordiale_ was looked
2725𓀴upon as a Socialist blunder to be
 sharply counteracted in private by a
2726𓀴studied coldness of manner.
2727𓀴
2728𓀴Mrs. Pinsent, whose
 French the whole
 family relied upon, did nothing to
2729𓀴improve the situation.
 She said to
 Madame la Comtesse de Brenteuil, who
2730𓀴couldn’t very well
 help going up in
 the lift with her, “Isn’t it a pity
2731𓀴the Vatican shuts
 so often for church
 things? They say we sha’n’t be
2732𓀴able to get into
 the Sistine chapel
 in Holy Week, and one of my
2733𓀴daughters is writing an article
 on the Sibyls--it’s really most
2734𓀴annoying!”
2735𓀴
2736𓀴Madame de Brenteuil
 looked at Mrs.
 Pinsent as if she were a smut that
2737𓀴had fallen on her sleeve; then,
 with a weary irony, she observed,
2738𓀴“Perhaps, Madame,
 the English do not
 realize that the Holy Father is a
2739𓀴Catholic?” Mrs. Pinsent was eager
 to reassure her as to Anglo-Saxon
2740𓀴intuitions. She
 said, “Oh, yes--we
 quite understand his own personal
2741𓀴views--but it isn’t as if Rome
 really belonged to him, is it?”
2742𓀴
2743𓀴Fortunately the lift
 stopped. It was
 not Madame de Brenteuil’s _étage_,
2744𓀴but she got out.
2745𓀴
2746𓀴After this incident
 no French person
 in the Le Roy spoke to Mrs. Pinsent
2747𓀴or her daughters, so that it was
 rather difficult for Léon Legier to
2748𓀴begin--especially
 as he was a third
 cousin to the Comtesse, and _lié_ to
2749𓀴almost everybody
 there. He had made
 up his mind to begin from the moment
2750𓀴that Rose Pinsent
 dropped a breakfast
 roll and blushed as she stooped to
2751𓀴pick it up.
2752𓀴
2753𓀴He had never seen
 such a blush before
 on any woman’s face, and any color
2754𓀴he had failed to surprise
 upon a woman’s
 face he had naturally supposed
2755𓀴could not exist.
2756𓀴
2757𓀴Apparently it did,
 for Rose had it.
 Her blush was as fine in hue as that
2758𓀴of a pink tulip and as delicate
 as a winter cloud at dawn.
2759𓀴
2760𓀴It swept up in a
 wave from her white
 throat into her pale, silky, fair
2761𓀴hair, and the fact
 that she suddenly
 discovered Léon was observing her
2762𓀴did not tend to
 decrease her color.
 Léon Legier made his opportunity
2763𓀴that evening in the
 hall. The porter
 was explaining to Mrs. Pinsent what
2764𓀴time to start for
 Tivoli the following
 morning. His English was limited
2765𓀴and he altered the train hour to
 suit the convenience of the foreign
2766𓀴tongue. The greater inconvenience
 of missing the train had not occurred
2767𓀴to him until Léon intervened.
2768𓀴
2769𓀴Subsequently Léon discovered that
 almost all the porter’s other
2770𓀴information suffered from similar
 readjustments of language, and he and
2771𓀴Mrs. Pinsent sat
 down in the lounge
 to revise the day’s excursion. Mrs.
2772𓀴Pinsent should, perhaps,
 have thought
 of her daughters, but Léon gave
2773𓀴her no time to think
 of her daughters.
 He focused her attention upon
2774𓀴herself. She felt
 herself young again,
 almost dangerous; the young man
2775𓀴before her apologetic, diffident,
 with exquisite manners, was so
2776𓀴obviously attracted
 by her and intent
 on all that she had to tell him,
2777𓀴she had not the heart to cut the
 conversation short. Later on Mr.
2778𓀴Pinsent joined them.
 He was delighted
 to find another man to talk to in
2779𓀴his own tongue, and
 who was obviously
 acquainted with the name of Lloyd
2780𓀴George.
2781𓀴
2782𓀴It fortunately never
 transpired that
 Léon had confused the name of the
2783𓀴then Chancellor of the Exchequer
 with that of a horse who had won the
2784𓀴Derby.
2785𓀴
2786𓀴Mr. Pinsent told Léon
 what the English
 Government really meant and
2787𓀴attacked the Italian
 railway system.
 Léon listened politely, and it was
2788𓀴only at the end
 of the conversation
 that Mr. Pinsent discovered his
2789𓀴young foreign friend
 was after all
 merely French. Not that Mr. Pinsent
2790𓀴minded Frenchmen--but they could
 hardly be held responsible for the
2791𓀴state of Italian railways.
2792𓀴
2793𓀴In spite of his nationality,
 however,
 Léon was able to give Mr. Pinsent
2794𓀴the name of a remarkably good
 wine to be procured at Tivoli; he
2795𓀴regretted that the best place to
 lunch required a slight knowledge of
2796𓀴how to order Italian
 dishes. Mr. Pinsent
 said it was a pity Léon wasn’t
2797𓀴going with them.
 Léon only hesitated
 enough not to appear over-eager;
2798𓀴his deprecatory,
 half-delighted eyes
 sought Mrs. Pinsent’s, and she said
2799𓀴quickly, “But perhaps
 you could come
 with us?” Léon produced his card.
2800𓀴The Pinsents gave him theirs
 on which was written “Rocketts,
2801𓀴Thornton-in-the Hedges,”
 and on Léon’s
 was written, No. 9, Faubourg St.
2802𓀴Germain, Paris, and there was no
 one there to point out the deadly
2803𓀴disparity between
 the two addresses.
2804𓀴
2805𓀴
2806𓀴                  
             CHAPTER II
2807𓀴
2808𓀴The next day was glorious,
 a spacious,
 sunny, golden Roman day. The air
2809𓀴across the Campagna
 was delicate and
 keen. The Cascades at Tivoli fell
2810𓀴in tumultuous rushes out
 of their purple caverns.
2811𓀴
2812𓀴The tiny temple of
 the Sibyls caught
 itself up against the sharpness of
2813𓀴the sky. It hung, perilous
 and delicate,
 above the cliff like a little
2814𓀴weather-beaten storm-tossed
 shell.
2815𓀴
2816𓀴Léon made no attempt
 to talk to Rose.
 He hardly looked at her, but he
2817𓀴persistently observed her. He saw
 that she had in some sense which had
2818𓀴been denied her family,
 imagination and warmth.
2819𓀴
2820𓀴He surprised in her a sympathy of
 attention different from the playful
2821𓀴kindness of her sisters.
2822𓀴
2823𓀴Agatha and Edith
 were from the first
 jolly with Léon. They cultivated,
2824𓀴with masculine acquaintances,
 a slightly
 jocular tone, and the humor of
2825𓀴this was deepened, of course,
 by Léon’s being a Frenchman.
2826𓀴
2827𓀴They gambolled cheerfully about
 him, much like heavy sheep dogs
2828𓀴good-humoredly greeting
 a greyhound.
2829𓀴
2830𓀴Léon accommodated
 his pace to theirs,
 he met them half-way, but he
2831𓀴privately thought they
 weren’t like women at all.
2832𓀴
2833𓀴He took Agatha round the little
 temple and Edith to the foot of the
2834𓀴highest waterfall.
 He let their niceness
 (for he recognized with a rare
2835𓀴leap of the imagination that they
 were being nice to him) expand into
2836𓀴unconscious revelations.
2837𓀴
2838𓀴He allowed their
 frank communications
 to slip over the polished surface
2839𓀴of his manner like
 leaves borne on
 the waters that dashed past them.
2840𓀴Once or twice he arrested
 the floating
 leaves with the point of his
2841𓀴stick, and once or
 twice in the flood
 of Edith’s careless chatter he
2842𓀴held, very slightly,
 the point of his
 mind against a special revelation.
2843𓀴
2844𓀴He gathered that
 the Pinsents were
 very well off. It wasn’t that they
2845𓀴ever spoke of money,
 but the tremendous
 amount of things you had to
2846𓀴have, the bother of _salonslits_
 and the burden of expensive hotels
2847𓀴filtered through their
 wider statements
 of having to think twice as to
2848𓀴whether they’d go on to Sicily or
 not. They deplored prices, but they
2849𓀴invariably chose the best of what
 there was. Léon listened carefully,
2850𓀴but he hadn’t at first
 any real intentions.
2851𓀴
2852𓀴Rose pleased him.
 She was an unknown
 English type. A strange creature,
2853𓀴as independent as
 if she were married,
 and as innocent as if she had
2854𓀴never seen a man.
2855𓀴
2856𓀴He decided to devote
 himself a little
 to studying her, and in order to
2857𓀴do this he had, of course,
 to accept the Pinsent family.
2858𓀴
2859𓀴To Mr. Pinsent he could
 only be attentive.
 He found him an English club
2860𓀴and was delighted to
 observe the increasing
 use which Mr. Pinsent made
2861𓀴of it. Mrs. Pinsent, however, was
 comparatively easy to handle. She was
2862𓀴a woman with the
 maternal instinct,
 and with her Léon found it easy to
2863𓀴be candid.
2864𓀴
2865𓀴He told her that
 he had just finished
 his military service and was now
2866𓀴taking a little “voyage”
 before settling
 down. He talked a good deal
2867𓀴about his mother,
 who occupied herself
 with good works in Paris; his
2868𓀴father he mentioned less, and the
 works that occupied him not at all.
2869𓀴Nevertheless, it
 could be seen that
 he had a great affection for both
2870𓀴his parents and no
 brothers and sisters.
2871𓀴
2872𓀴“I expect that’s
 why he likes,” Mrs.
 Pinsent explained to her husband,
2873𓀴“to be so much with the girls.”
2874𓀴
2875𓀴It was three days before Léon
 found himself alone with Rose.
2876𓀴
2877𓀴She had begun to
 feel a little out
 of this gay stranger’s intimacy. It
2878𓀴seemed to Rose as
 if Léon purposely
 avoided her, and yet, in a way,
2879𓀴which was very strange
 to her, their
 eyes sometimes met, and then he
2880𓀴seemed to be telling
 her something
 as direct as a penny and as
2881𓀴articulate as the
 cobblestones of Rome.
2882𓀴
2883𓀴Then all of a sudden,
 breathlessly,
 without preparation, she found
2884𓀴herself alone with
 him in the Campagna.
2885𓀴
2886𓀴Mr. Pinsent had
 said that the girls
 were on no account to go outside the
2887𓀴walls of Rome by themselves.
2888𓀴
2889𓀴He hadn’t made it perfectly clear
 why he put this obstacle to their
2890𓀴general freedom,
 but he’d mentioned
 when pressed by Agatha, malarial
2891𓀴fever and savage
 Abruzzi sheep dogs.
2892𓀴
2893𓀴“So I expect I shall just have
 to go to a gallery instead,” Rose
2894𓀴explained to Léon
 in the hall. “Edith
 has gone off with some one to see
2895𓀴a fountain, and won’t be back for
 hours.” Léon hesitated, then he said,
2896𓀴“How far does the
 wonderful English
 freedom extend? Is it an
2897𓀴impertinence that
 I should offer to
 take you--wherever you wish to go?”
2898𓀴
2899𓀴“Oh, thank you very much. Yes, of
 course I could go with you. . . .”
2900𓀴Rose answered a little
 slowly. It seemed
 to her in some strange way that
2901𓀴her freedom had ceased
 to be menaced
 by her father and mother, but not
2902𓀴to have become any
 the more secure.
2903𓀴
2904𓀴She couldn’t have said
 that she disliked
 the sense of danger, but she
2905𓀴knew quite well
 what increased it.
 It was Léon’s saying as they stood
2906𓀴for a moment outside
 the street door,
 “Do you know it is since three
2907𓀴days--I have been
 waiting for this?”
2908𓀴
2909𓀴They took the tram
 to San Giovanni
 Laterano, and as it shuffled and
2910𓀴shrieked its clamorous
 way through
 narrow streets and wide piazzas,
2911𓀴under old yellow
 walls and through
 long white modern tunnels, a new
2912𓀴sensation came to Rose Pinsent.
2913𓀴
2914𓀴She had always supposed that what
 she liked best in a man was his being
2915𓀴tremendously manly, and by manly
 the Pinsents meant impervious to the
2916𓀴wills of others,
 abrupt in speech,
 and taking up everywhere a good deal
2917𓀴of space.
2918𓀴
2919𓀴But Léon was masculine in quite a
 different way and yet no one could
2920𓀴doubt his possessing
 that particular quality.
2921𓀴
2922𓀴The form it took
 with him was that
 Rose became suddenly conscious of his
2923𓀴physical presence. She noticed as
 she had never noticed in any man
2924𓀴before, his smallest
 personal habits,
 the flutter of his fine hard eyes,
2925𓀴the scrupulous neatness and grace
 of his person, and, above all, the
2926𓀴alert and faultless
 precision with
 which in any direction he met her
2927𓀴half-way.
2928𓀴
2929𓀴He gave her from moment to moment
 the whole of his indulgent intensity.
2930𓀴No man had ever
 looked at her like
 this before, so read her mind and
2931𓀴forced her in return
 to read his own!
2932𓀴
2933𓀴The tram was crowded
 and Léon stood
 above her, holding on to a strap and
2934𓀴looking down at her with laughing
 eyes. “You are thinking something of
2935𓀴me, Mademoiselle,”
 he said at last.
 “Confess it is a comparison, not, of
2936𓀴course, to my advantage.
 Tell me, then,
 to whom are you comparing me, in
2937𓀴what do I fall short?”
2938𓀴
2939𓀴Rose tried to frown. “Why should
 you suppose I am thinking of you at
2940𓀴all?” she ventured.
 Léon laughed softly.
 “Why indeed?” he murmured. “And
2941𓀴yet why should you not? Here you
 are, you and I; we have not yet
2942𓀴exchanged half-a-dozen
 words, and now
 we are to be together for, I hope,
2943𓀴three hours, and all these last
 days I have been waiting for these
2944𓀴hours--planning for
 them, arranging,
 as it were, my life to meet them.
2945𓀴Surely you, who
 have not prevented
 my obtaining them, must now be giving
2946𓀴a thought to what
 I am like? It would
 be droll to go for a ride on a
2947𓀴strange horse and not to look
 at it, not question a little its
2948𓀴character, how shall
 we put it--its
 pace? Would you think less of the
2949𓀴companionship of a man?”
2950𓀴
2951𓀴Rose drew in her
 breath sharply. Léon
 had a way of putting things which
2952𓀴was very exciting, but
 not, perhaps, quite nice.
2953𓀴
2954𓀴“But,” she said,
 “of course we have
 thought of you--Mother and Father,
2955𓀴they thought you
 were--” she paused,
 breathless. Léon came to her
2956𓀴assistance. “Respectable?
 Oh, yes,”
 he said easily. “But that doesn’t go
2957𓀴very far, does it?
 Simply to go for
 an expedition with some one who is
2958𓀴respectable! Your
 excellent Mr. Thomas
 Cook could provide you with that.
2959𓀴You might even procure
 for a few francs
 more a gentleman to give you a
2960𓀴lecture! Really, Mademoiselle,
 I had flattered myself that your
2961𓀴imagination had dealt with
 me a trifle more directly!”
2962𓀴
2963𓀴Rose tacitly admitted this claim.
 “Agatha and Edith thought you awfully
2964𓀴jolly,” she said hurriedly. “So I
 didn’t see, I mean I didn’t mind when
2965𓀴you suggested coming
 out with me.”
2966𓀴
2967𓀴Léon laughed again.
 “But I am afraid,”
 he said, “that I sha’n’t be in
2968𓀴the least with you
 what I was like
 with Mademoiselles Agatha and
2969𓀴Edith--‘awfully jolly.’
 I do not think
 of you in those terms. You will
2970𓀴have to decide for
 yourself and not
 take anybody’s word for it what I am
2971𓀴like to you.”
2972𓀴
2973𓀴Rose said nothing.
 She was glad that
 they had to get out at the foot of
2974𓀴the Lateran steps.
2975𓀴
2976𓀴They took a little carriage which
 went very fast through the swollen,
2977𓀴sallow suburb; it
 soon left behind
 it the trams, the cobblestones and
2978𓀴the shuffling wine carts. Almost
 at once the Campagna was upon them,
2979𓀴vaguely breaking
 away from the farms
 and the eucalyptus trees into
2980𓀴soft-breathing, deep,
 unbroken emptiness.
2981𓀴
2982𓀴They wandered out over the grass
 to the ruin of a villa, an old pink
2983𓀴tower and a group
 of umbrella pines.
2984𓀴
2985𓀴“It asks to be sketched,
 doesn’t it?”
 Léon observed. “And now you will
2986𓀴have to be very definite,
 Mademoiselle.
 It won’t do for you to suppose
2987𓀴that you can judge
 of the Campagna
 without a glance, as if it were
2988𓀴merely a new masculine
 acquaintance!”
2989𓀴
2990𓀴He opened her camp-stool
 and gravely
 placed himself on an old wall
2991𓀴behind her. “Vous y êtes,”
 he asserted, “begin!”
2992𓀴
2993𓀴But Rose didn’t
 begin. She had been
 thinking of what he had asked her.
2994𓀴Perhaps she hadn’t
 been quite frank.
 The Pinsents as a family thought it
2995𓀴a sin not to be quite frank.
2996𓀴
2997𓀴“I _was_ thinking about
 you,” she admitted.
 “I mean myself; I thought--I
2998𓀴thought you weren’t at
 all like an Englishman!”
2999𓀴
3000𓀴Léon laughed gently.
 “What a discovery,”
 he said. “I am not like an
3001𓀴Englishman--I! And did you want
 me to be? You are disappointed,
3002𓀴perhaps?”
3003𓀴
3004𓀴The wonderful pink
 color deepened in
 her face. “No,” she said, “I am not
3005𓀴a bit disappointed--I like
 people to be different.”
3006𓀴
3007𓀴“Thank you, Mademoiselle,”
 he said
 with sudden gravity. “You relieve me
3008𓀴very much, for that
 is one thing I
 could not change for you--I could not
3009𓀴be less a Frenchman.”
3010𓀴
3011𓀴Still she did not
 begin her sketch.
 “You are tired?” he asked her. “Rest
3012𓀴then, and don’t trouble to make
 a picture on that little strip of
3013𓀴canvas. Nothing you can do there
 will, I assure you, be half as
3014𓀴successful as what you are doing,
 by just sitting where you are.”
3015𓀴
3016𓀴She was sure he was flirting now,
 but what she wasn’t sure was how to
3017𓀴stop it. She wondered
 if Edith or Agatha
 knew, but it flashed across her
3018𓀴in a terrible moment
 of disloyalty
 that perhaps neither of them had ever
3019𓀴been put to the proof. “I don’t
 think you ought to say that kind of
3020𓀴thing--” she said a
 little uncomfortably.
3021𓀴
3022𓀴“But why not?” Léon
 urged. “Why do
 you not wish me to take pleasure in
3023𓀴your beauty? And
 if I take it, would
 it not be rude and ungracious not
3024𓀴to express it? For
 my part I believe
 only in the truth. If it is
3025𓀴agreeable--good!
 Let us enjoy it. If
 it is disagreeable, let us bear it.
3026𓀴But why should we
 try to avoid it?
 Besides we can never avoid it. If we
3027𓀴choose to shut our
 eyes to the truth
 it will take us by surprise. Is
3028𓀴that the way you like to
 be taken, Mademoiselle?”
3029𓀴
3030𓀴Rose was not a stupid
 girl; she gave
 Léon a fleeting glance; there was
3031𓀴just the delicate
 hint of laughter
 in it, her lips trembled at the
3032𓀴upturned corners.
 “I don’t like being
 taken at all,” said Rose sedately,
3033𓀴returning to her sketch.
3034𓀴
3035𓀴It occurred to her
 afterwards this
 was not, perhaps, the best way to
3036𓀴stop flirting.
3037𓀴
3038𓀴They came back rather
 late for tea,
 but Mr. and Mrs. Pinsent were not at
3039𓀴all uneasy about
 them. They didn’t
 look feverish and they hadn’t seen
3040𓀴any savage Abruzzi dogs.
3041𓀴
3042𓀴
3043𓀴                  
            CHAPTER III
3044𓀴
3045𓀴For a Frenchman
 of the type of Léon
 Legier there are a great many ways
3046𓀴of being in love; there are also
 several goals. You needn’t, as he
3047𓀴himself expressed it, finish the
 game in order to have received your
3048𓀴entertainment.
3049𓀴
3050𓀴In the case of Rose Pinsent, Léon
 wasn’t at first very serious, he was
3051𓀴out for the fine shades. He had
 never had an intimacy with an
3052𓀴Englishwoman before.
 It was simply
 a nationality he didn’t know, and he
3053𓀴found it touching.
3054𓀴
3055𓀴For her sake he led the Pinsents
 in compact and cheerful batches into
3056𓀴unknown churches and gave them on
 unfrequented hillsides splendid Roman
3057𓀴views.
3058𓀴
3059𓀴He never made a visible point of
 the few moments alone he managed to
3060𓀴snatch with Rose.
3061𓀴
3062𓀴She took these moments
 with a certain
 unexacting grace which pleased
3063𓀴him.
3064𓀴
3065𓀴It was as if she had a special
 pleasure which never amounted to
3066𓀴expectation in his
 presence. Her grave
 blue eyes never claimed him, but
3067𓀴when he signaled his own joy into
 hers--he met with no rebuff. He had
3068𓀴passed certain barriers
 with her and
 she made no attempt to set them up
3069𓀴again.
3070𓀴
3071𓀴She was secretly
 afraid, not of him,
 but of being so different when they
3072𓀴were alone together.
 She tried very
 hard to be just the same as she was
3073𓀴when those queens
 of chaff, Agatha
 and Edith, presided over their small
3074𓀴festivities.
3075𓀴
3076𓀴She had never supposed
 before you could
 have two relations with the same
3077𓀴person, without doing
 anything wrong,
 and yet the most rigid of her
3078𓀴scruples failed to warn her that
 when she and Léon were together they
3079𓀴did anybody any harm.
3080𓀴
3081𓀴Rose would have stopped
 all “nonsense”
 at once; what she couldn’t stop
3082𓀴was the gradual dangerous
 tenderness,
 growing touch by touch under the
3083𓀴hand of a master.
3084𓀴
3085𓀴She tried not to
 think too much about
 Léon, and as long as he was with
3086𓀴them she found that
 she succeeded.
3087𓀴
3088𓀴Everything became
 so interesting and
 so vivid--but when Léon was out of
3089𓀴their sight, buried
 in obscure private
 affairs, hidden, perhaps, by his
3090𓀴French relations
 whom he persistently
 excused to the Pinsents as being
3091𓀴poor dear people, so
 terribly provincial
 and shy! Rose found Rome
3092𓀴wonderfully little
 of an absorption--she
 was forced to consider that
3093𓀴what she really needed
 was, like her
 sisters, some definite active goal.
3094𓀴Her mind became set upon a hobby.
 She felt if she had that, it wouldn’t
3095𓀴really matter whether
 Rome was interesting
 or not. She could not have
3096𓀴told quite how the idea came to
 her; perhaps it was because little
3097𓀴Italian children in the streets
 looked so sweet--but she suddenly
3098𓀴thought she would like, when she
 got back to England, to have a nice
3099𓀴little home in the
 country for children
 to get well in, quite poor
3100𓀴people’s children--only
 they would
 be washed there, of course, and
3101𓀴probably have curly

3102𓀴in St. Maria in Trastevere and

3103𓀴themselves into the

3105𓀴
3106𓀴“Papa,” Rose explained

3107𓀴be managed.” For a moment Léon

3108𓀴ducks--and then he

3110𓀴

3114𓀴
3115𓀴It seemed as if Rose’s

3117𓀴
3118𓀴She said quickly

3119𓀴such things.” Léon said, “Doesn’t

3120𓀴
3121𓀴He hadn’t said any

3122𓀴saw as clearly as

3123𓀴definite new life.
3124𓀴
3125𓀴This mere study of

3126𓀴the most serious

3127𓀴
3128𓀴A girl as beautiful

3130𓀴an elastic pocket

3131𓀴favorable opportunity.
3132𓀴
3133𓀴After all, he meant

3134𓀴his father’s extravagance

3136𓀴but she wasn’t eager--she made no

3139𓀴
3140𓀴She was the only

3142𓀴
3143𓀴Léon had been very

3144𓀴not a large town, nor one in

3145𓀴acquaintances definitely apart.
3146𓀴
3147𓀴Léon was at this

3148𓀴was the Pinsent affair--which

3149𓀴deal of time, and

3150𓀴there was another

3151𓀴less serious, of course, but also

3154𓀴

3156𓀴without notifying

3157𓀴Pinsent. Mr. Pinsent decided that

3159𓀴got there--but what

3161𓀴
3162𓀴It was at the Grand

3163𓀴coming inexorably

3165𓀴
3166𓀴He notified the

3167𓀴instantly retire in

 His companion stared, not at
3168𓀴him--a glance had

3169𓀴Pinsent family. She said under

3170𓀴families--they have tribes--this

3171𓀴
3172𓀴Léon never moved a muscle of his

3174𓀴hotel--where he

3175𓀴follow him there--it

3176𓀴There are forms that must be

3177𓀴self-interest. Léon knew that he

3178𓀴Pinsents went any

3181𓀴been too much for

3187𓀴denied steadily that he was ever

3188𓀴could he have been there and not

3189𓀴conceivable--they

3190𓀴much wealth of detail the lady he

3191𓀴so merrily into

3192𓀴
3193𓀴Léon looked graver

3194𓀴asked, “were you under the

3195𓀴
3196𓀴“It certainly did

3197𓀴rather troubled. “I particularly

3198𓀴
3199𓀴“Lots of people wear

 Legier,” Rose said, looking away
3200𓀴from Léon.
3201𓀴
3202𓀴She was the only one of the party

3203𓀴
3204𓀴He did more than
 admire her then, he
 respected her. There is no taste so
3205𓀴perfect as that which permanently
 conceals a fact which is awkward for
3206𓀴others.
3207𓀴
3208𓀴Rose concealed it, but she paid

3209𓀴
3210𓀴
3211𓀴                  
             CHAPTER IV
3212𓀴
3213𓀴Léon planned in
 advance the setting
 for his proposal. He would make it
3214𓀴in the English way, to the girl
 herself. Léon had never proposed
3215𓀴marriage before, and he gave

3216𓀴
3217𓀴The Baths of Caracalla are never
 very crowded and at certain times of
3218𓀴the day they are extraordinarily
 solitary.
3219𓀴
3220𓀴Léon knew one of
 the chief excavators
 and it was part of his idea to
3221𓀴take the entire family of Pinsent
 with the exception of Rose into the
3222𓀴underground regions.
 The excavator,
 who was an enthusiast, could be
3223𓀴calculated to hold them there for

3224𓀴underground temples,
 agreed easily

3225𓀴Léon disappeared with the others.
 She was a little puzzled over
3226𓀴Baedeker’s description
 of the Baths
 of Caracalla, once she got the
3227𓀴tepidarium in her

 could get on quite easily, but the
3228𓀴tepidarium eluded her. The great
 roofless, sunny space, wouldn’t
3229𓀴contract itself

3230𓀴returning footsteps.
3231𓀴
3232𓀴“Has anything happened?”
 she asked in some alarm.
3233𓀴
3234𓀴“That is for you

3235𓀴part I have found you--and that

3236𓀴
3237𓀴“Didn’t you mean to stay down

3238𓀴bewilderment.
3239𓀴
3240𓀴“Never in the world,”

3241𓀴engage myself with the

3242𓀴beg your pardon--I

3243𓀴have no use for him!”
3244𓀴
3245𓀴There was no one

3246𓀴pink walls stretched

3247𓀴overhead, under foot

3249𓀴

3252𓀴“Let us leave propriety

3253𓀴much in need of

3254𓀴the shelter of the

3255𓀴you had not informed

3256𓀴have told that tree--that it was

3257𓀴complexion; but as

3258𓀴favor with any one, I should

3259𓀴

3263𓀴
3264𓀴A silence fell between

3265𓀴became aware that she was alone

3266𓀴never been alone

3267𓀴danger. In a moment

3270𓀴
3271𓀴Then she knew that

3274𓀴

3276𓀴their candor.
3277𓀴
3278𓀴“No!” she whispered. “No!”
3279𓀴
3280𓀴“_Alors!_ You will not be able to

3281𓀴bending towards

3287𓀴such a brute that

3288𓀴world! That is no

3289𓀴say ‘No’ to me, Rose?--for your

3301𓀴

3329𓀴

3332𓀴

3334𓀴
3335𓀴Rose nodded. “Because,”

3337𓀴

3343𓀴

3358𓀴
3359𓀴He drew a long breath,

3360𓀴

3370𓀴
3371𓀴It was as serious as death.
3372𓀴
3373𓀴
3374𓀴                  
             CHAPTER V
3375𓀴
3376𓀴The Hotel le Roy
 became a place for

3377𓀴everybody else. The

3378𓀴even the lift became

3379𓀴course none of them had the least

3380𓀴engagement, had taken

3381𓀴_convenable_ and also

 His French relatives were
3382𓀴furious. He let

3383𓀴when he had to see them, that

3384𓀴charming.
3385𓀴
3386𓀴The Pinsents were

3387𓀴but they didn’t like it.
3388𓀴
3389𓀴Mr. Pinsent made

3390𓀴was out of the question.
 Mrs. Pinsent

3391𓀴himself and talking

3392𓀴the one plank of

3393𓀴demanded. Mrs. Pinsent

3394𓀴merely said, “I’m

3395𓀴Mr. Pinsent knew what that meant.
 If he saw Rose he was lost. But as a
3396𓀴matter of fact, he

3397𓀴had lost him.
3398𓀴
3399𓀴After Mr. Pinsent had finished

3400𓀴nonsense and that

3401𓀴perfectly right, but it wasn’t as

3402𓀴was really not at

3403𓀴Léon was most obliging,

3404𓀴all,” she finished,

3405𓀴me himself he wasn’t

3406𓀴
3407𓀴In the end Mr. Pinsent had to see

3408𓀴further interview with Léon.
3409𓀴
3410𓀴The interview was

3411𓀴have been.
3412𓀴
3413𓀴Mr. Pinsent had no

3415𓀴He walked up and down

3417𓀴how much he would

3418𓀴three girls, and her golf

3419𓀴
3420𓀴He said he thought

3421𓀴He hadn’t seen any

3422𓀴stayed in the Rue

3423𓀴Rose would like

3424𓀴quite different from being abroad

3426𓀴sentimentalized

3427𓀴
3428𓀴Emotion should take place (enough

3429𓀴Léon and Rose; it

3430𓀴and as for talking

3431𓀴could have been

 You simply, of course, didn’t talk of
3432𓀴it. Mr. Pinsent, however, did.
3433𓀴
3434𓀴“Of course we must

3437𓀴
3438𓀴Then Mr. Pinsent shook hands with

3440𓀴

3446𓀴

3457𓀴

3465𓀴
3466𓀴She could buy what

3468𓀴

3474𓀴
3475𓀴“We sha’n’t want to be gay,” Rose
 said a little soberly. “We shall just
3476𓀴be perfectly happy.”
3477𓀴
3478𓀴Léon said no more.
 Of course he expected
 to be happy, but he had never
3479𓀴in his life been happy when
 he wasn’t a little gay.
3480𓀴
3481𓀴Rose saw very little
 of Léon during
 their brief engagement. They were
3482𓀴both immersed in preparations for
 the wedding, but the little she saw
3483𓀴was like the vision
 of a Fairy Prince.
3484𓀴
3485𓀴He was gallant, delicate
 and intent.
 Nothing about Rose escaped him. He
3486𓀴knew with a marvelous
 tact from moment
 to moment what would please her
3487𓀴best.
3488𓀴
3489𓀴It was (but of course Rose didn’t
 know this) the correct attitude for a
3490𓀴Frenchman engaged to be married.
3491𓀴
3492𓀴As the marriage approached, Mrs.
 Pinsent had moments of secret doubt.
3493𓀴She knew it was
 very silly of her,
 but Rose was her youngest child, and
3494𓀴marriage by two consuls
 and a Cardinal
 wasn’t at all like being married
3495𓀴properly in your
 own church at home.
3496𓀴
3497𓀴She went so far one evening as to
 go into Rose’s bedroom under the
3498𓀴pretext of borrowing
 her hairbrush,
 just to see if her child was quite
3499𓀴happy. Mrs. Pinsent’s
 hair was long
 and thick like Rose’s, it had been
3500𓀴the same color when
 she was Rose’s
 age. She sat in an armchair by the
3501𓀴bed and thought that Rose, whose
 hair was done in two long plaits,
3502𓀴looked terribly like she used to
 look when she was ten years old.
3503𓀴
3504𓀴“My dear,” she said, “I like Léon
 so much.” Rose smiled and blushed and
3505𓀴snuggled further
 into the rather hard
 second pillow reluctantly conceded
3506𓀴to her by the Hotel le Roy.
3507𓀴
3508𓀴“Yes, Mamma, I know,” she said,
 “and he loves you--isn’t it nice?”
3509𓀴
3510𓀴Mrs. Pinsent reflected.
 “All the same,”
 she said, “men are very strange.
3511𓀴I mean even our own
 men. You’d think
 you could tell what they’re like
3512𓀴before you are married
 to them, but
 you can’t--you don’t even know for
3513𓀴quite a long time afterwards.”
3514𓀴
3515𓀴Rose looked unconcerned. “It’s so
 funny,” she said, “but I feel as if I
3516𓀴knew Léon better than if he was
 an Englishman. You see, he tells me
3517𓀴more. I can’t quite
 put it to you,
 so that you can understand, but I
3518𓀴think it’s his being so
 much more expressive.”
3519𓀴
3520𓀴“Yes,” said Mrs. Pinsent. “Only
 that isn’t what I mean, you know. I
3521𓀴wasn’t thinking
 of what they said,
 any of them. I don’t think you can go
3522𓀴by that; when they’re in
 love, they’ll say anything.”
3523𓀴
3524𓀴Rose hesitated. “But, Mamma,” she
 said, “don’t men--don’t they _ever_
3525𓀴stay in love?”
3526𓀴
3527𓀴Mrs. Pinsent resorted
 hastily to the
 hairbrush. Almost all married women
3528𓀴dislike this question.
3529𓀴
3530𓀴“Of course, in a sense,”
 she admitted.
 “But when they get used to you
3531𓀴they aren’t always
 very easy to hold.”
3532𓀴
3533𓀴Rose sat up very
 straight and slim.
 “How do you mean--_hold_?” she asked
3534𓀴quickly. Mrs. Pinsent brushed her
 hair well over her face. She hoped
3535𓀴Rose wasn’t thinking
 about her father.
 It was an unnecessary fear, Rose
3536𓀴wasn’t thinking about
 any one but Léon.
3537𓀴
3538𓀴“Well,” Mrs. Pinsent
 explained, “I
 think there comes a time in almost
3539𓀴all happy marriages
 when a man has
 almost too much of what he wants. He
3540𓀴gets, if one isn’t
 very careful, and
 perhaps even if one is--a little--a
3541𓀴little restive and
 bored. You see,
 men never have as much to amuse
3542𓀴themselves with
 as women have--and
 that makes them take more interest in
3543𓀴what they _do_ like
 even if it isn’t
 good for them--and other women
3544𓀴(whom they wouldn’t
 really care for
 a bit--if they saw enough of them)
3545𓀴may make an appeal
 to them just because
 they’re _not_ their wives. Of
3546𓀴course, it mayn’t
 be at all like this
 with Léon, dear, only you’re going
3547𓀴so far away from us--and
 he’s a Frenchman,
 and perhaps they don’t think
3548𓀴of marriage quite
 as we do. I have
 never read Zola, of course, but I
3549𓀴believe there is
 rather a difference
 in the point of view.” Mrs. Pinsent
3550𓀴faltered--she felt
 through the cascade
 of her hair--Rose’s inflexible
3551𓀴eyes.
3552𓀴
3553𓀴“What would you do,
 Mamma,” Rose asked
 quietly, “if anything--happened
3554𓀴like that?”
3555𓀴
3556𓀴Mrs. Pinsent drew
 a long breath. For
 a moment she was almost sorry that
3557𓀴Bernard Shaw hadn’t had a sharper
 effect upon her daughter’s
3558𓀴imagination. Mrs. Pinsent
 wasn’t anxious
 to explain what she would do.
3559𓀴She only wanted
 to be vague, and at
 the same time helpful; her own case
3560𓀴had been quite different,
 there had
 been the children, and, besides, Mr.
3561𓀴Pinsent wasn’t French.
3562𓀴
3563𓀴“We rather thought,”
 she said, “of
 staying on for some time in Rome, and
3564𓀴then going to Paris
 for the first part
 of the summer. We should be quite
3565𓀴near you then--and Agatha could
 go back to England for her tennis.”
3566𓀴
3567𓀴“I couldn’t ever
 leave Léon,” Rose
 said strangely, “whatever happened.”
3568𓀴
3569𓀴“No, dear, of course not,” said
 Mrs. Pinsent soothingly, then she
3570𓀴started quite afresh and
 began plaiting her hair.
3571𓀴
3572𓀴“Your father wanted
 me to tell you,”
 she said, “that he’s going to have
3573𓀴your allowance settled
 upon you--and
 upon your children--that’s £500 a
3574𓀴year, and later
 on you’ll have even
 more, of course, like your sisters,
3575𓀴but the money is
 in an English bank,
 and it is quite your own, but
3576𓀴you’re to have trustees
 as well, your
 father has seen to all that. Léon
3577𓀴was so nice about it. I knew he
 would be. He’s been so generous and
3578𓀴charming and most
 thoughtful.” Mrs.
 Pinsent got up and bent over her
3579𓀴daughter. “You are happy, Rose?”
 she whispered. “You do feel safe?”
3580𓀴
3581𓀴Rose lifted her undeterred,
 terribly
 triumphant eyes to her mother’s. “I
3582𓀴feel as safe,” she said,
 “as if an angel loved me.”
3583𓀴
3584𓀴
3585𓀴                  
             CHAPTER VI
3586𓀴
3587𓀴Everything had been
 done, the last
 trunk was packed, the last joke, not
3588𓀴a very good one, accomplished by
 Agatha. The two elder sisters, tired
3589𓀴out and unequal to their natural
 play of spirits, had gone to bed.
3590𓀴
3591𓀴Rose flew downstairs
 to the telephone.
 The Swiss manageress, a
3592𓀴sharp-tongued, good-hearted
 woman,
 rose wearily and shouted through the
3593𓀴receiver. After
 a violent exchange
 of reproaches with an irate porter at
3594𓀴the other end, she
 accomplished the
 feat of getting hold of Léon, and
3595𓀴put the receiver into
 the girl’s hand.
 “He is there, Mademoiselle,” she
3596𓀴said with a curious glance
 at the girl’s flushed face.
3597𓀴
3598𓀴“Oh, thank you,” Rose murmured.
 “Léon, Léon, are you there?”
3599𓀴
3600𓀴“But it is Rose?”
 His voice answered
 a little as if he was surprised
3601𓀴that it was Rose.
3602𓀴
3603𓀴“Yes,” she said quickly, “I want
 to see you. Can you come at once?”
3604𓀴
3605𓀴“Something has happened?”
 he asked
 anxiously. “Something has gone
3606𓀴wrong?”
3607𓀴
3608𓀴Rose reassured him.
 “Oh, no--nothing,
 but I felt suddenly as if I _must_
3609𓀴see you.”
3610𓀴
3611𓀴There was a moment’s
 pause, a buzzing
 sound came across the wires, and
3612𓀴then Rose heard
 a strange voice--it
 sounded like a woman’s saying very
3613𓀴slowly, “_Mais--c’est la dernière
 nuit?_” And then Léon’s again, “I am
3614𓀴very busy to-night,
 Rose--this that
 you want to see me about, is it
3615𓀴important?”
3616𓀴
3617𓀴She was surprised
 at his hesitation,
 and surprised at her own
3618𓀴insistence. It seemed
 to her suddenly
 very important that she should
3619𓀴insist. “Please,
 please come,” she
 said urgently. There was another
3620𓀴pause, then Léon said
 again, “Is it a command?”
3621𓀴
3622𓀴A moment earlier
 she would not have
 said that it was a command, but her
3623𓀴wish to see him had
 been mysteriously
 sharpened into a strange
3624𓀴imperative instinct.
3625𓀴
3626𓀴“Isn’t my wish a
 command?” she asked,
 trying to laugh. But Léon did not
3627𓀴echo her laughter. “Very well,
 then,” he said, “in ten minutes.”
3628𓀴
3629𓀴The big red salon
 was empty. For the
 first time Rose noticed the yellow
3630𓀴lamp, the blue velvet tablecloth,
 the enormous imperishable roses in
3631𓀴bulging angular vases under
 the great gilt mirror.
3632𓀴
3633𓀴She had been so happy all these
 weeks she hadn’t really seen what
3634𓀴anything was like,
 and she had hardly
 ever been alone for ten minutes.
3635𓀴Now she was alone.
 She remembered with
 a little smile that Léon had once
3636𓀴said of the salon that as an
 interior it was not seductive.
3637𓀴
3638𓀴The Pinsents did
 not use irony, but
 Rose thought she rather liked it. In
3639𓀴ten minutes precisely
 Léon was with
 her. Fortunately Madame de Brenteuil
3640𓀴had gone to bed.
3641𓀴
3642𓀴Léon entered quickly,
 looking about
 him as if he had expected one or
3643𓀴more of the Pinsent family to be
 in attendance. Only Rose, feeling
3644𓀴suddenly rather
 small and very far
 away, stood under an imitation palm
3645𓀴close by the mantelpiece.
3646𓀴
3647𓀴Léon took her hands, kissed them,
 pressing them, and letting them go in
3648𓀴one quick movement.
3649𓀴
3650𓀴“I am here,” he said,
 drawing a seat
 up close to her. “Well--what is
3651𓀴this thing that
 has suddenly become
 necessary for us to talk about?”
3652𓀴
3653𓀴Rose looked at him questioningly.
 Really she hardly knew what it was
3654𓀴that she wanted to
 see him for, perhaps
 it was after all only to see
3655𓀴him! To count over her riches, to
 feel the wonderful golden coins slip
3656𓀴through her eager
 fingers. Only now
 as she met his eyes it seemed to her
3657𓀴that he shut her out.
 He had a strange
 hard look, and though he smiled,
3658𓀴his smile itself
 had a new quality,
 a quality which seemed to put her a
3659𓀴little to one side.
 “I don’t quite
 know, Léon,” she murmured. “I did
3660𓀴want to see you--but I think
 I must have had some reason.”
3661𓀴
3662𓀴Léon glanced through
 the glass door
 of the salon at the back of the
3663𓀴Manageress’ head.
 “Let us hope so,”
 he said cheerfully, “for it is ten
3664𓀴o’clock and I see no one here
 but Madame at the Bureau.”
3665𓀴
3666𓀴“Father was here--but
 I sent him away,”
 Rose explained conscientiously.
3667𓀴
3668𓀴Léon gave an odd little laugh.
 “To-night,” he said, “you are very
3669𓀴imperative. But you see we are
 all your slaves. He went--I
3670𓀴came--well--what
 do you wish of us?”
3671𓀴
3672𓀴“Léon,” she whispered,
 frightened by
 the coldness of his voice, “weren’t
3673𓀴you glad to come?”
3674𓀴
3675𓀴He gave himself
 a tiny shake as if
 he were trying to pull himself into a
3676𓀴fresh frame of mind.
3677𓀴
3678𓀴“But of course,”
 he said, “you are
 adorable.” To a critical ear his tone
3679𓀴lacked conviction, but Rose’s ear
 was not critical; that is to say, not
3680𓀴yet. She gave a little
 sigh of relief.
3681𓀴
3682𓀴“I think I know
 what it was I meant
 to say,” she stated, “Mamma has been
3683𓀴talking to me about marriage.”
3684𓀴
3685𓀴“Ah--!” said Léon quickly.
3686𓀴
3687𓀴“Something she said,”
 Rose continued,
 “made me wonder. You see, I had
3688𓀴always supposed when you were in
 love--that was enough. But what she
3689𓀴said made me wonder if perhaps it
 didn’t matter a good deal _how_?”
3690𓀴
3691𓀴Léon looked a trifle puzzled, but
 he was also amused, his hardness was
3692𓀴beginning to melt under the spell
 of her wistful loveliness;
3693𓀴something--some other spell,
 perhaps, receded from him.
3694𓀴
3695𓀴“_Bien sur_,” he
 murmured, looking
 into her eyes. “It matters how one
3696𓀴loves.”
3697𓀴
3698𓀴“And I couldn’t help
 thinking,” Rose
 went on with gathering confidence,
3699𓀴“that you knew rather
 more about it than I do.”
3700𓀴
3701𓀴Léon’s eyes flickered
 under the yellow
 lamps. It was almost as if they
3702𓀴were laughing at her.
3703𓀴
3704𓀴“Yes,” he said caressingly,
 “yes--that is always possible.”
3705𓀴
3706𓀴“You see,” Rose explained,
 “all along
 I have felt as if you knew me, and
3707𓀴what I wanted, and how you could
 please me, so astonishingly well.”
3708𓀴
3709𓀴Léon smiled. He did not tell her
 that compared to other women--many
3710𓀴other women--she
 was easy to please.
3711𓀴
3712𓀴“Of course,” Rose went on, “in a
 way I understand you. I told Mamma
3713𓀴that! Better than
 if you were English,
 because we’ve talked so much, you
3714𓀴see--but I’m not sure--not quite
 sure--that I know all the things you
3715𓀴don’t like.
3716𓀴
3717𓀴“What I wanted to
 ask you to-night
 was--will you always tell me what you
3718𓀴want and not mind
 if I’m stupid and
 don’t know things until you tell me?
3719𓀴You need never tell me more than
 once--I shall always remember.”
3720𓀴
3721𓀴She had touched him
 now, touched him
 so much that he sprang to his feet
3722𓀴and walked hastily
 to the window. She
 could not see his face. She waited
3723𓀴patiently and a little
 anxiously for
 him to come back to her. He said,
3724𓀴when he came back, and
 stood behind her chair:
3725𓀴
3726𓀴“You are adorable,”
 but he said it
 quite differently, he said it as if
3727𓀴he really found her
 adorable. “It is
 true,” he said at last, very gently
3728𓀴and tenderly. “There are things
 that we must teach each other, and
3729𓀴to-night I will teach
 you one of them.
 You should not have sent for me
3730𓀴here.”
3731𓀴
3732𓀴“Ah, but why, Léon?”
 she cried. “It
 was just the last night”--her voice
3733𓀴faltered--some queer little trick
 of the brain forced into her memory
3734𓀴the voice she had heard
 on the telephone.
 That woman, too, had said to
3735𓀴somebody that it
 was the last night.
3736𓀴
3737𓀴“In the first place,”
 he said, still
 gently, but a little gravely, “you
3738𓀴should not have
 seen me at all--on
 the evening before our marriage, it
3739𓀴is the reason itself! You should
 have spent it with your mother and
3740𓀴sisters. It surprised
 me--it surprised
 me very much--your sending for
3741𓀴me.”
3742𓀴
3743𓀴She flushed crimson.
 “Do not think
 I blame you,” he said quickly. “But I
3744𓀴am a Frenchman,
 and you must learn
 a little how we think.” Rose bowed
3745𓀴her head. “And in
 the second place,”
 he said, “my very dear child--you
3746𓀴must not constrain me to come to
 you--it is my delight--my joy to be
3747𓀴with you--be very
 careful that you
 never make it my duty! I am your
3748𓀴lover--to-morrow I shall be your
 husband. So--so you will remember,
3749𓀴never try to constrain
 me to be with
 you--let me come, let me go, do not
3750𓀴try to hold me, and do not seek
 to know where I have been.”
3751𓀴
3752𓀴“But,” she cried
 eagerly, “Léon--I
 didn’t mean to do anything like that!
3753𓀴I--I was frightened.
 I wanted you!
 Just to see you! I never will
3754𓀴again--I mean--I don’t
 think--do you?--I
 shall ever be frightened again.
3755𓀴It wasn’t that I
 meant to--oh, what
 a horrible word--constrain you--only
3756𓀴I thought you would be alone
 and wanting me, too!”
3757𓀴
3758𓀴“Mon Dieu!” he cried, with sudden
 exasperation. “Of course I want you!”
3759𓀴
3760𓀴She drew back a little
 from the savage
 light in his eyes--he had caught
3761𓀴her arm suddenly and roughly--but
 in an instant he had himself in hand.
3762𓀴“Now I am going,”
 he said. “You are
 not to be frightened any more. You
3763𓀴are mine, my sweetheart, my wife,
 my darling! How I love the pretty
3764𓀴English words!--and you will love
 a little your funny French husband,
3765𓀴will you not?--and
 forgive him, if
 you do not always understand him.”
3766𓀴
3767𓀴He took her very
 gently in his arms,
 and kissed her troubled eyes and
3768𓀴put his lips lingeringly
 and tenderly
 to hers. There were tears on her
3769𓀴eyelashes, but she smiled bravely
 up at him. “I will never forget what
3770𓀴you have said,” she murmured,
 “and I will love you always.”
3771𓀴
3772𓀴Then he went away.
 After he had gone,
 it occurred to Rose that she was
3773𓀴to belong to him,
 but if they were
 to be happy he must not belong to
3774𓀴her. She did not put it quite as
 sharply as this, but she reminded
3775𓀴herself that the great thing was
 for Léon never to feel bound.
3776𓀴
3777𓀴Madame came in from
 the bureau to put
 out the lights. “You will not need
3778𓀴them any more, Mademoiselle,” she
 asked, “now that Monsieur has gone?”
3779𓀴
3780𓀴“No,” said Rose. “Thank you very
 much. Madame, are you French?”
3781𓀴
3782𓀴“No, Mademoiselle,”
 the Manageress
 replied. “I am a Swiss from Basle.”
3783𓀴
3784𓀴“But you know French
 people?” Rose insisted.
3785𓀴
3786𓀴Madame shrugged her
 shoulders. “I know
 most people,” she observed. “Even
3787𓀴Arabs, I once kept
 a hotel in Egypt;
 but why do you ask, Mademoiselle?”
3788𓀴
3789𓀴“I wondered,” Rose
 said, “if you thought
 them--the French, I mean--very
3790𓀴difficult to please?”
3791𓀴
3792𓀴“No people are easy
 to please,” Madame
 replied, putting out the lights
3793𓀴with a sharp twist,
 as if she disliked
 them. “And all are unpleasant
3794𓀴when they are not
 pleased. I do not
 say the French are more unpleasant
3795𓀴than the others.
 They know what they
 are about and they don’t ask for
3796𓀴the moon and expect to get it for
 two sous, but what they ask for--that
3797𓀴they do expect to
 get no matter what
 it costs others that they should
3798𓀴have it. In general,
 I find the French
 have very little heart. I have no
3799𓀴complaint to make against them.
 They are orderly, they do not waste
3800𓀴time, they have
 the sense of how to
 behave. But I find it is better to
3801𓀴expect nothing from
 them, and to remain
 independent. Is there anything
3802𓀴further you require,
 Mademoiselle?”
3803𓀴
3804𓀴Rose thanked her again and turned
 thoughtfully away. Madame, with the
3805𓀴last switch in her
 hand, looked curiously
 after her. “The English,” she
3806𓀴said to herself,
 “are not practical.
 Nevertheless, Madame de Brenteuil
3807𓀴is quite wrong about
 them. They mean
 no harm. The whole family Pinsent
3808𓀴walks about with
 its eyes shut, as
 innocent as the newly baptized. They
3809𓀴are a race of mystics
 without manners.
 It is what comes of a meat
3810𓀴breakfast so early
 in the morning.
 The senses become clogged. I must not
3811𓀴forget to remind Alfonso that the
 father Pinsent wants bacon with his
3812𓀴eggs.”
3813𓀴
3814𓀴
3815𓀴                  
            CHAPTER VII
3816𓀴
3817𓀴They had been married a week--a
 tremulous, ecstatic, amazing week.
3818𓀴
3819𓀴It seemed to Rose made up of all
 the laughing colors of the sea.
3820𓀴
3821𓀴They were surrounded
 by the sea, clear
 and limpid as a shallow pool, the
3822𓀴great deep bay gleamed
 and shone about them.
3823𓀴
3824𓀴Out of it the Islands
 rose like flowers.
 Capri uneven, wild and blue,
3825𓀴Ischia tulip-shaped
 and tall--Posilippo
 and its attendant isles like a
3826𓀴fallen spray of blossoms;
 and in Capri
 itself the whole spring lay bare
3827𓀴to the sun.
3828𓀴
3829𓀴The South was like Léon--it was
 beautiful, but it was strange.
3830𓀴
3831𓀴On their first evening
 they had driven
 swiftly up the hillside; the air
3832𓀴was cold and keen;
 the small mountain
 ponies galloped through the
3833𓀴quick-falling darkness
 and just for
 a moment a breath of fear touched
3834𓀴the triumphant bride.
3835𓀴
3836𓀴She longed for something
 familiar,
 something that wasn’t even beautiful,
3837𓀴but to which she had
 grown accustomed.
 She didn’t put it to herself
3838𓀴quite like that--she
 only wished she
 hadn’t had to leave her fox terrier
3839𓀴at home.
3840𓀴
3841𓀴The moment passed and other
 richer moments took its place.
3842𓀴
3843𓀴Love was just--what
 without expecting
 it Rose had most desired. No one
3844𓀴could have expected any one to be
 as wonderful as Léon. He spilt his
3845𓀴soul into his passion,
 his ardor filled
 their hours, there was no way in
3846𓀴which he did not
 color her life. She
 felt herself like some poor common
3847𓀴pebble transformed
 into purple and
 rose color by the touch of the sea.

 aside and let me mix myself up in
4221𓀴it.”
4222𓀴
4223𓀴She shivered a little.
 “Oh, but why,”
 she asked, “should you be mixed up
4224𓀴in it? We only saw
 them yesterday!”
4225𓀴
4226𓀴Léon picked up the hat again. “It
 appears,” he said, “that I managed to
4227𓀴entertain Madame yesterday.
 Poor thing!

4228𓀴a tortured Romantic.

4229𓀴smile, and he became attracted by

4230𓀴amuse her a little, she would be

4231𓀴she might submit her case to me,

4236𓀴happiness?”

4238𓀴Rose thought the

4239𓀴and there her heart

4240𓀴Léon was magnificent,

4248𓀴
4249𓀴She shut her eyes

4250𓀴but he felt a little

4251𓀴but there was no doubt that their

4256𓀴
4257𓀴He kissed her joyously.
 “There,” he

4260𓀴day I shall bring you the history

4262𓀴resource, and I

4264𓀴
4265𓀴Rose did not share the feeling.
 She picked up the hat which he had
4266𓀴finished and tried

4267𓀴does it really suit me, Léon?”
4268𓀴
4269𓀴“Ah, you must make it suit you,”
 said Léon a little ironically. “I
4270𓀴cannot help you to that! It’s all

4271𓀴

             CHAPTER IX
4274𓀴
4275𓀴Léon had, from the

4276𓀴comrade to Raoul, the sympathetic

4277𓀴situation in general

4278𓀴rainbow in the heavens!
4279𓀴
4280𓀴His shrewdness of

4282𓀴idle. Rose had not

4284𓀴
4285𓀴Léon was on the look-out

4286𓀴training, efforts

4291𓀴turn. Madame Gérard

4292𓀴childhood and a sentimental and

4293𓀴point from experience.
4294𓀴
4295𓀴All her adventures

4297𓀴brought her up very

4299𓀴Gérard’s impression of what

4300𓀴
4301𓀴He had spoilt her

4302𓀴her life; but he

4303𓀴trees walking, even Léon was only

4305𓀴notice of him, her

4308𓀴

4313𓀴refined his flatteries.

4317𓀴woman. She remembered Rose.
4318𓀴
4319𓀴“We must not neglect

4323𓀴Also Rose was learning

4324𓀴third in their little

4325𓀴them.
4326𓀴
4327𓀴“She has a pride about it,” Léon

4329𓀴
4330𓀴“I should not have

4331𓀴“She has kind eyes.

4332𓀴
4333𓀴Léon led the conversation back to

4334𓀴Rose’s eyes. He

4335𓀴think of her as cold

4386𓀴

4398𓀴

4438𓀴

4459𓀴
4460𓀴Madame Gérard was the only one of

4462𓀴had chosen for them.

4464𓀴
4465𓀴The good intentions

4468𓀴
4469𓀴                  
             CHAPTER X
4470𓀴
4471𓀴It was part of their

4472𓀴Rose was never to

4473𓀴
4474𓀴Léon and Madame tore

4476𓀴after a prolonged but

4477𓀴

4481𓀴

4492𓀴pillow-shaped and glittering in a

4493𓀴from its porch,
