4580𓀴English type? Even
 Monsieur Gérard
 laughed at this, but on the whole
4581𓀴Madame spared Rose;
 she kept as far
 as possible her hand off her. She
4582𓀴would gladly have spared
 her altogether,
 and, in a sense, of course, she
4583𓀴was doing so. She
 was giving her her
 husband back--not wiser, nor more
4584𓀴appreciative, and certainly in a
 far worse state of mind--but for all
4585𓀴that he would be returned to Rose
 this afternoon not so very much the
4586𓀴worse for wear, as husbands go.
4587𓀴
4588𓀴For half-an-hour
 Madame Gérard took
 upon her little supple shoulders the
4589𓀴entertainment of
 her guests. She was
 for that half hour like the whole
4590𓀴cast of the Comédie Française put
 together--brilliant, exquisitely
4591𓀴decorative and incredibly,
 ironically
 knowing; then she turned to her
4592𓀴husband with her eyes
 like an innocent
 caress, and said, “Now, _mon
4593𓀴ami_, will you not
 make music for us?”
 Monsieur Gérard was not unwilling
4594𓀴to use his magnificent
 gift. Léon,
 who felt that the end had come,
4595𓀴politely echoed the
 request; and then
 Madame made her fatal mistake. The
4596𓀴game was hers--she
 had only to stand
 aside and let it finish itself; but
4597𓀴she could not stand
 aside--nervously,
 with happy flutterings, she must
4598𓀴show them how she followed
 her husband’s
 work, and how she helped him:
4599𓀴and she didn’t help him at all.
4600𓀴
4601𓀴She drew out his music--it wasn’t
 what he wanted to sing and he said so
4602𓀴crisply; he always
 knew what he wanted
 to sing. Then she said she must
4603𓀴play his accompaniment,
 so that he
 could stand up and let his voice out.
4604𓀴
4605𓀴Now Monsieur Gérard’s
 voice was not
 of a quantity to be lightly let out
4606𓀴in a small bird-cage
 of a room; it
 would have been sufficient to roll
4607𓀴over Capri like a
 rock-stream. Also,
 Monsieur Gérard was like a tiger to
4608𓀴any accompanist but
 his own, who was
 taking at the moment a much-needed
4609𓀴holiday.
4610𓀴
4611𓀴It counted for nothing
 at all with
 Monsieur Gérard that his wife was
4612𓀴dressed in white
 and scarlet and gold
 and that she had roused in him the
4613𓀴temporary sentiments
 of attraction.
 From the moment that she mounted the
4614𓀴music-stool nothing counted but
 her power of playing a correct
4615𓀴accompaniment without
 too much expression.
 She had evoked the artist,
4616𓀴and the artist upsets everything.
4617𓀴
4618𓀴Monsieur Gérard began to sing; he
 modulated his great dramatic voice,
4619𓀴but the sound of
 it shook the Villa
 degli Angeli; it poured out on the
4620𓀴dancing air with
 the majestic roll
 of great billows breaking on the
4621𓀴beach.
4622𓀴
4623𓀴Madame tinkled mildly
 and prettily
 on the piano after him--too prettily
4624𓀴of course, and not
 very accurately.
 The little ineffective notes were
4625𓀴like a pee-wit chirping
 in a storm.
 In an instant Monsieur Gérard had
4626𓀴swept her from the
 music-stool almost
 on to the floor. “You have no more
4627𓀴music in you than
 a fly!” he broke
 off abruptly to inform her, then he
4628𓀴sat down in her place and roared
 in velvet with magnificent effect.
4629𓀴
4630𓀴Madame, shaken and
 reduced from triumph
 to the verge of tears, quivered
4631𓀴for a little in the window-seat;
 but even then her prize was still
4632𓀴within her grasp--Monsieur
 had simply
 for the moment forgotten her. She
4633𓀴was capable, if
 she had waited, of
 reminding him successfully. Alas! she
4634𓀴had that fatal longing
 to help which
 reduces the greatest women to the
4635𓀴level of a nuisance.
 She could not
 let herself be forgotten even for a
4636𓀴moment--even for
 his art. She would
 go back and turn over the leaves for
4637𓀴Raoul. He frowned,
 he swore under his
 breath, he shook his heavy head at
4638𓀴her; but she went on turning over
 the leaves--he was not playing to the
4639𓀴score, he did not want his leaves
 turned over--her eager, fluttering
4640𓀴figure drove him frantic.
 In ten minutes
 he banged the piano lid down,
4641𓀴and threw the score
 on the carpet.
 He told her before Léon, before Rose,
4642𓀴in the drawing-room
 of the Villa degli
 Angeli that she was an intrusive
4643𓀴insect!
4644𓀴
4645𓀴There was a horrible
 pause. Léon approached
 Madame in a state of mingled
4646𓀴chivalry and satisfaction.
 She was
 a pitiable figure as she stood there
4647𓀴biting at her dainty
 lace handkerchief
 to keep the tears back; her face
4648𓀴was very white under its layer of
 powder. Probably it would have been
4649𓀴better if she had
 sat down. She simply
 stood with imploring, helpless
4650𓀴eyes fixed upon the angry
 tyrant before her.
4651𓀴
4652𓀴No angry man likes
 to be looked at
 helplessly. Monsieur Gérard glared at
4653𓀴her--then he made the gulf that
 had come between them impassable.
4654𓀴
4655𓀴“Understand!” he shouted, turning
 the music still on the piano to and
4656𓀴fro, as if he were
 making hay, “To-night
 I go to Naples! I cannot stand
4657𓀴more of this! I will
 go to Naples for
 one night, for two--for three! You
4658𓀴must remain here!
 I go to Naples!”
 Madame’s eyes went from her husband
4659𓀴to Léon. Léon’s
 eyes were fixed on
 hers in pity, in forgiveness--and
4660𓀴were they also fixed
 a little in expectation?
 He knew, and Madame knew,
4661𓀴precisely the purpose of Monsieur
 Gérard in going to Naples.
4662𓀴
4663𓀴Rose did not understand
 as much as
 this, but she thought it was very
4664𓀴wicked of Monsieur
 Gérard to go away
 from his wife on their honeymoon
4665𓀴because she tried to turn
 over the leaves of his music.
4666𓀴
4667𓀴She got up and crossed
 the room towards
 Madame. It was Rose who put an
4668𓀴end to the unendurable silence.
4669𓀴
4670𓀴Léon was waiting for
 a cue from Madame,
 and Madame was too stunned to
4671𓀴give him any cue.
 She was like a little
 helpless leaf that has brought
4672𓀴on its own storm, but Rose waited
 for nothing. She looked first at
4673𓀴Monsieur Gérard.
 She compelled that
 enraged artist to meet her steady,
4674𓀴disapproving eyes;
 then she held out
 her hand to Madame Gérard and with
4675𓀴a gracious diffidence
 that was the
 perfection of dignity, she said in
4676𓀴her stumbling French,
 “I hope very
 much, Madame, that if your husband is
4677𓀴to leave you for
 a few days, you will
 give us as much of your company as
4678𓀴possible.”
4679𓀴
4680𓀴Madame excused herself.
 She murmured
 under her breath that Rose was too
4681𓀴kind. Once more
 her eyes flickered
 from her husband to Léon. “It is a
4682𓀴great happiness to me to second
 my wife’s invitation,” said Léon
4683𓀴gravely. He murmured
 something more
 as he bowed over her hand and kissed
4684𓀴it. Rose had already
 turned and without
 even glancing in the direction
4685𓀴of Monsieur Gérard she went
 out into the gay little garden.
4686𓀴
4687𓀴Capri saw them return
 to the Hotel Paradiso.
4688𓀴
4689𓀴Léon was remorsefully
 attentive to
 his wife; he treated her as if she
4690𓀴were something very valuable
 that might break.
4691𓀴
4692𓀴Perhaps in some
 subconscious way he
 knew that he was going to break her,
4693𓀴but he was very much impressed
 by her behavior.
4694𓀴
4695𓀴She was, he thought
 to himself, the
 soul of generosity, and when we are
4696𓀴sure that we have the
 soul of generosity
 to deal with we sometimes find
4697𓀴it difficult not to
 take advantage of it.
4698𓀴
4699𓀴
4700𓀴                  
             CHAPTER XI
4701𓀴
4702𓀴The next day Monsieur
 Gérard carried
 out his intention of going to
4703𓀴Naples.
4704𓀴
4705𓀴Madame Gérard remained invisible.
 She accepted the flowers Léon called
4706𓀴upon her to present,
 but she sent down
 a message that she was indisposed
4707𓀴and could see nobody. She was
 indisposed until five o’clock the
4708𓀴following day. By this time
 she had made up her mind.
4709𓀴
4710𓀴It was not an easy task. She said
 to herself again and again that she
4711𓀴would have accepted
 heartbreak--but
 she could not accept outrage. Her
4712𓀴husband had not only
 cruelly wronged
 her--he had done so publicly before
4713𓀴the eyes of a man
 who loved her--and
 before his wife. Her marriage was a
4714𓀴false step--it had been her first
 adventure--but in her imagination she
4715𓀴had only counted
 upon adventures as
 successes--now she was face to face
4716𓀴with an adventure
 which had proved
 a failure. She could not go back--she
4717𓀴could only go on--and
 yet she hesitated,
 for after marriage, adventures
4718𓀴that go on are no
 longer innocent.
 Her husband had left her with a
4719𓀴weapon lying within
 her reach--from
 the first it had occurred to her
4720𓀴that she could strike back with
 Léon, but with this idea had come
4721𓀴another one, that
 in striking back
 she must cruelly wound an innocent
4722𓀴and happy woman.
 In all the horrible
 scene which had taken place the day
4723𓀴before there had
 only been one moment
 less intolerable than the others,
4724𓀴and Rose had given
 her that moment.
 She had distinctly stood by her with
4725𓀴an offer of friendship.
4726𓀴
4727𓀴Madame Gérard spent twenty-four
 bitter, sleepless hours considering
4728𓀴Rose. At the end
 of that time--having
 come to the decision that she did
4729𓀴not want to hurt
 her, but that she
 wished to do the thing that would
4730𓀴hurt her--she made
 the further decision
 that, after all, it need not
4731𓀴hurt Rose so very much. When she
 thought of her own unhappiness, a
4732𓀴little distress on
 the part of other
 wives did not seem out of place.
4733𓀴
4734𓀴She would do her
 best to shield Rose
 from the truth, but she wouldn’t do
4735𓀴anything to prevent
 the truth taking
 place. These two decisions placed
4736𓀴her in a better position than
 Léon. Léon had decided nothing.
4737𓀴
4738𓀴He only knew that
 he must see this
 complex woman, that he must, out of
4739𓀴chivalry, discover what she felt
 about the incredible behavior of her
4740𓀴husband. He must
 find out also--in
 honor or common kindness--if there
4741𓀴wasn’t in the situation
 some successful
 part for a good friend to play.
4742𓀴He drew upon all his virtues for
 his reasons. Yesterday Madame had
4743𓀴sharply wounded
 his _amour propre_;
 he saw that she had been playing a
4744𓀴game with him. Well, the game had
 failed, and yet he was still there;
4745𓀴there was therefore
 still the possibility
 of a new game under new
4746𓀴conditions, with the advantage,
 perhaps, to him.
4747𓀴
4748𓀴He went no further than that. He
 wanted, he assured himself, to go no
4749𓀴further. He was full
 of consideration
 for Rose, but he distinctly wished
4750𓀴to see how far he could go.
4751𓀴
4752𓀴At five o’clock he found
 himself admitted.
 Madame was already out in the
4753𓀴sheltered wistaria-covered
 balcony.
 She lay in a long chair draped in a
4754𓀴soft white robe;
 there were pearls
 round her neck and a little black
4755𓀴velvet band. She looked
 extraordinarily
 pathetic and young and very
4756𓀴tired of grief.
4757𓀴
4758𓀴There were no traces
 of tears on her
 little white face--but she was not
4759𓀴the woman to allow traces of any
 kind to appear, unless they were
4760𓀴becoming.
4761𓀴
4762𓀴“It was kind of you to come,” she
 said gently after a long pause.
4763𓀴“Forgive me, I had misjudged you.
 I thought that you were playing with
4764𓀴me.”
4765𓀴
4766𓀴Léon protested eagerly,
 how could she
 have had such an idea? One did not
4767𓀴go about playing with
 young and innocent
 women who were unhappy. She
4768𓀴must not do him
 so much injustice.
4769𓀴
4770𓀴He talked for five minutes nobly
 and eloquently about unhappy young
4771𓀴married women. Madame
 Gérard listened,
 looking between the wistaria
4772𓀴branches towards the sea. When he
 had quite finished she said gently,
4773𓀴“And yet it was
 a plot between you
 and my husband--your friendship, your
4774𓀴attention to me--they
 were not very
 real, Monsieur. You had agreed with
4775𓀴him to win me over to his
 wishes. Is that not so?”
4776𓀴
4777𓀴Léon was upset. You can never be
 sure what a husband will not tell a
4778𓀴wife, even an estranged and angry
 husband. There is a terrible habit of
4779𓀴indiscriminate confidence
 in marriage.
 Léon had come across it before.
4780𓀴
4781𓀴He would have eagerly
 denied conjecture,
 but it would not do to deny a
4782𓀴confidence; besides
 he was secretly
 much relieved at this new version of
4783𓀴things. He had been
 afraid that Madame
 had been playing with him; it
4784𓀴appeared now that
 he had been playing
 with her. What had happened
4785𓀴yesterday was merely
 a charming little
 feminine _revanche_. He began to
4786𓀴find the part he was
 playing more attractive.
4787𓀴
4788𓀴“It is true,” he
 said at last, “your
 husband told me that your marriage
4789𓀴was not happy--and
 to begin with perhaps
 I had the idea that it lay with
4790𓀴you to make it so.
 Forgive me, this
 idea soon passed. It passed before
4791𓀴the affair of the
 other day showed
 me the incredible _lacheté_ of Raoul.
4792𓀴Permit me to say
 that his behavior
 shocked me to the heart; but before
4793𓀴this shock took
 place I had learned
 in what light to consider you.
4794𓀴Believe me, I have
 not been playing
 with you. I am in earnest, in
4795𓀴terrible earnest.”
4796𓀴
4797𓀴She turned her eyes to his. They
 were not beautiful eyes like
4798𓀴Rose’s--but he did
 not know them so
 well, besides she used them better.
4799𓀴“You are really
 in earnest, really,
 Léon?” she asked him searchingly. He
4800𓀴sprang to his feet,
 but with a wave
 of her hand she motioned to him to
4801𓀴remain where he was.
4802𓀴
4803𓀴“I wonder,” she said very softly.
 “I do not want to be twice deceived,
4804𓀴to be deceived once
 is to go broken-winged
 through life, but to be
4805𓀴deceived twice, could
 one live at all?”
4806𓀴
4807𓀴“I swear that I have not deceived
 you--that I will never deceive you!”
4808𓀴cried Léon passionately.
 “The feeling
 that I have for you is real--it is
4809𓀴intense.”
4810𓀴
4811𓀴Still he meant to stay at Capri;
 he hadn’t any idea of doing anything
4812𓀴else.
4813𓀴
4814𓀴“You are prepared,”
 she asked him,
 “to prove your words to me? You
4815𓀴realize if I believe them what is
 at stake for me--and if you realize
4816𓀴that, do you not
 think that I have
 the right to ask you for a proof?”
4817𓀴
4818𓀴“You shall not ask
 me for one!” he
 cried. “Rather I will give you _all_
4819𓀴the proofs in my power--one
 or a dozen--what
 you will--you have only to
4820𓀴ask!”
4821𓀴
4822𓀴“You are very generous,” she said
 with her pretty irony. “One will be
4823𓀴enough. I want you
 to-night to take
 me to Naples. I cannot stay in Capri
4824𓀴until my husband
 returns. I will not
 return alone to France. It appears
4825𓀴that we made a mistake
 in not going
 to Naples for our honeymoon. Let us
4826𓀴then--you and I--rectify
 this mistake.”
4827𓀴
4828𓀴Léon said nothing.
 He gripped at the
 little wooden balcony railing with
4829𓀴both hands, and stared
 with blank eyes
 at the laughing sea. Leave Capri!
4830𓀴Leave Rose! His heart
 shuddered within
 him--with every honest fiber of
4831𓀴his nature, and
 he had many honest
 fibers in his nature, he loved Rose.
4832𓀴He did not love the woman before
 him--but he had sought what she
4833𓀴offered--how could
 he refuse it? It
 was true he had expected to make his
4834𓀴own terms, but this
 would not be very
 easy to explain to her. Still, he
4835𓀴tried hard to keep
 the situation in hand.
4836𓀴
4837𓀴“I have said,” he began at last,
 “that I considered your husband, in
4838𓀴leaving you, to
 have committed the
 worst of infamies. You are asking me
4839𓀴to commit the same.”
4840𓀴
4841𓀴Madame raised her eyebrows.
4842𓀴
4843𓀴“You mean in leaving
 your wife?” she
 asked. “After what you have allowed
4844𓀴me to suppose, I
 had not thought you
 would have that feeling. Nor would
4845𓀴it be necessary for you to act
 as my husband has acted. But I am
4846𓀴supposing, of course, that what
 you feel for me is--real.”
4847𓀴
4848𓀴“Pardon me, Madame,”
 said Léon firmly,
 “all that I have said to you is
4849𓀴true--and yet--is it incredible
 to you?--I love my wife!”
4850𓀴
4851𓀴Madame smiled at him.
4852𓀴
4853𓀴“You know how children
 play with daisies?”
 she said. “As they pull off
4854𓀴the little white petals
 one by one--‘He
 loves me--a little, very much,
4855𓀴passionately, not
 at all.’ It is funny
 what comes after passionately--so
4856𓀴soon after, Léon.”
4857𓀴
4858𓀴He stirred uneasily.
 Madame began to
 pull to pieces a spray of wistaria,
4859𓀴throwing the blossoms
 one by one smilingly
 into her lap. “I do not ask
4860𓀴you, my friend,” she
 said slowly, “for
 the devotion of a lifetime--there
4861𓀴are hardly enough to go round of
 these blossoms--we must not stop at
4862𓀴passionately, must
 we--we must stop
 at not at all! I was thinking of
4863𓀴spending three days in Naples.”
4864𓀴
4865𓀴“And you would expect me to leave
 you in three days?” asked Léon
4866𓀴reproachfully. He watched
 her feverishly.
 A man must know what he is in
4867𓀴for. “In three days,”
 said Madame,
 throwing all the silvery mauve
4868𓀴blossoms with a quick
 little gesture
 over the balcony, “I should insist
4869𓀴upon your leaving
 me.” As she did this
 her small, firm hand touched his.
4870𓀴He caught it to
 his lips and kissed
 it fervently. The smile in her eyes
4871𓀴deepened.
4872𓀴
4873𓀴She supposed he must have stopped
 thinking of Rose, but he said again,
4874𓀴after a moment’s pause, “To leave
 her--to leave her--that seems somehow
4875𓀴very base!”
4876𓀴
4877𓀴“Then do not leave
 her,” said Madame
 wearily, withdrawing her hand.
4878𓀴“Break your word
 to me, it is very
 simple. I have no claim on you--I am
4879𓀴not your wife.”
4880𓀴
4881𓀴“You are everything in the world
 to me,” he said desperately. For the
4882𓀴moment he believed she was.
4883𓀴
4884𓀴She leaned forward a little.
4885𓀴
4886𓀴“After all,” she said, “your wife
 will not know why you go to Naples.
4887𓀴You have only to say
 you go on business.
 She is so innocent she will
4888𓀴believe you--you might even tell
 her that you are to act as my escort
4889𓀴back to my husband.
 She need not suffer.”
4890𓀴
4891𓀴Léon flung back his head. “But,”
 he stammered, his eyes filling with
4892𓀴sudden tears, “I
 cannot lie to Rose!
 She is not like that! I cannot lie
4893𓀴to her--it is as you say,
 she would believe me!”
4894𓀴
4895𓀴“Ah,” said Madame,
 “let us hope then
 that you can lie about women better
4896𓀴than you can lie to them! But you
 are making a mistake. It is very easy
4897𓀴to lie to us. All men
 have found it so.”
4898𓀴
4899𓀴He pushed her words
 away from him.
4900𓀴
4901𓀴“Elise,” he asked
 her suddenly, “do
 you care for me? This thing that you
4902𓀴are about to do, is
 it from your heart?”
4903𓀴
4904𓀴She rose and stood beside him.
4905𓀴
4906𓀴“I will give you the proof,”
 she said in a low voice.
4907𓀴
4908𓀴But still he was not satisfied;
 his eyes continued to question her.
4909𓀴
4910𓀴“It is from my heart,”
 she repeated
 firmly. He caught her to him and
4911𓀴kissed her, but it
 seemed to him even
 then as if he held something dead
4912𓀴in his arms, something which by
 no beat of the heart, by no single
4913𓀴spiritual response, met
 his. She gave him her lips.
4914𓀴
4915𓀴For a long moment
 he held her, then
 she withdrew herself and moved away
4916𓀴from him. “No more,”
 she said gently.
 “To-night I shall expect you. I
4917𓀴will meet you at the turn of the
 road by the Madonna of the Rocks.”
4918𓀴
4919𓀴She moved with him slowly towards
 the door. “_Voyons!_” she said before
4920𓀴they parted. “Don’t
 hurt her--don’t
 ever tell her--your young wife. She
4921𓀴is too good. A lie
 will cost you nothing.
 And, after all, if it was not
4922𓀴me--it would be some other woman
 soon--would it not? After all--” Her
4923𓀴voice faltered. Something in her
 wavered for a moment, something very
4924𓀴hard and deep, tried
 suddenly to melt.
 “After all,” said Léon gravely,
4925𓀴“this is the greatest
 proof I have
 to give. Take it as generously as I
4926𓀴give it!”
4927𓀴
4928𓀴She looked at him
 with strange eyes.
 “We are both about to be very
4929𓀴generous, are we
 not?” she said with
 a dry little smile. “_Eh bien!_
4930𓀴Love is short and
 marriage is long--all
 the better for love--which sees
4931𓀴its end.”
4932𓀴
4933𓀴Léon did not like
 this point of view.
 There was some truth in it, no
4934𓀴doubt, but it would have sounded
 better from the lips of a man. He
4935𓀴kissed her hands
 reproachfully. He
 could not think for the moment of
4936𓀴anything very beautiful
 to say about
 love, and Madame herself said no
4937𓀴more. She simply looked
 suggestively at the door.
4938𓀴
4939𓀴After he had gone she stood where
 he had left her, clenching and
4940𓀴unclenching her
 small, firm hands.
4941𓀴
4942𓀴“From my heart,” she whispered,
 “_Mon Dieu_--it appears so--from my
4943𓀴heart.”
4944𓀴
4945𓀴
4946𓀴                  
            CHAPTER XII
4947𓀴
4948𓀴Rose was finishing
 a letter to Agatha
 on the balcony. She found it
4949𓀴difficult to write
 to her sisters,
 they seemed so very far away.
4950𓀴
4951𓀴She was afraid, too,
 that they might
 find her letters dull. You couldn’t
4952𓀴go on describing the blue grotto;
 besides, neither Agatha nor Edith
4953𓀴cared for descriptions
 of scenery,
 they always skipped them in books;
4954𓀴and as far as Rose
 could tell nobody
 played any particular game in
4955𓀴Capri. Young men
 shot birds on Sunday
 afternoons when they could, but
4956𓀴they weren’t even
 the proper birds
 to shoot, so perhaps it was better
4957𓀴not to mention them.
4958𓀴
4959𓀴When Rose wrote to her people she
 always said “we” even when she was
4960𓀴referring to things that
 she did by herself.
4961𓀴
4962𓀴It wasn’t very like
 a Pinsent to give
 way to this illicit expansion of
4963𓀴fact, but Rose comforted herself
 by thinking that after all, editors
4964𓀴said “we” when there was only one
 of them writing, and most of the
4965𓀴married people she knew expressed
 themselves in the plural, though that
4966𓀴perhaps was because
 they really did
 the things together. Still, she went
4967𓀴on writing “we”
 because she didn’t
 want her people to think anything
4968𓀴funny about Léon.
 She had just got
 as far as “We have such jolly little
4969𓀴dinners in the garden,” when she
 heard Léon’s whistle coming up the
4970𓀴stairs. He stood looking
 at her a little curiously.
4971𓀴
4972𓀴“You are writing,” he asked
 her, “to your people?”
4973𓀴
4974𓀴“To Agatha,” she said.
 “Have you any message?”
4975𓀴
4976𓀴Léon sometimes sent
 very amusing messages
 to Agatha. For a moment Léon
4977𓀴did not reply, then he said, “And
 what do you say to them--of me--your
4978𓀴people?”
4979𓀴
4980𓀴Rose blushed, just
 the same wonderful
 pink tulip blush Léon had from the
4981𓀴first particularly
 admired, but it
 was ill-timed, it looked guilty. It
4982𓀴shot through his
 uneasy mind that she
 had been complaining of him to the
4983𓀴Pinsents. In his
 irritable, resentful
 state it gave him a sudden sense
4984𓀴of justification. Hadn’t he done
 already wonders for Rose? He had not
4985𓀴made open love to
 Elise (until just
 now, of course), he had borne for
4986𓀴over a month the ennui of Capri.
 He hadn’t so much as been to a café
4987𓀴without his wife, and now he had
 almost decided not to leave her!
4988𓀴
4989𓀴“Tell them,” he said
 bitterly, “that
 you are perfect, and that I am a
4990𓀴monster of depravity. Almost all
 wives say that to their relatives
4991𓀴sooner or later. You, it appears,
 have taken up the tone in good time!”
4992𓀴
4993𓀴“Léon!” she cried,
 aghast. And then,
 because she loved him so, because
4994𓀴she had shielded
 him in the defiance
 of truth, because she had never had
4995𓀴a suspicion of his faithlessness,
 she chose this moment to say the only
4996𓀴harsh thing she had
 ever said to him.
 “I think,” she said, turning away
4997𓀴her eyes, “that you are
 guilty of very bad taste.”
4998𓀴
4999𓀴It was, of course,
 the one fatal reproach
 to make to a Frenchman. If she
5000𓀴had said he was
 guilty of anything
 else he would have forgiven her.
5001𓀴
5002𓀴Léon rushed into their room, his
 cheeks on fire as if she had struck
5003𓀴him. It was clear
 she no longer loved
 him! Coldly, cruelly, with her
5004𓀴horrible English justice, so out
 of place in a woman, she had thrown
5005𓀴this stone at his
 heart! There could
 be but one issue now. He must go to
5006𓀴Naples. She complained
 of him to her
 parents and she had accused him of
5007𓀴bad taste! He packed
 a small bag feverishly.
 The door between them was
5008𓀴shut.
5009𓀴
5010𓀴Rose hesitated. Should she open
 it and tell him she was sorry?
5011𓀴
5012𓀴What would Agatha or Edith do, if
 they were there? Probably they would
5013𓀴have burst open the
 door with shouts
 of glee, and inserted a cake of
5014𓀴soap down Léon’s back, but this
 happy method of conciliation seemed
5015𓀴closed to Rose. She had never had
 their robuster gift of horseplay. She
5016𓀴got up hesitatingly
 and walked slowly
 away, out into the garden and
5017𓀴beyond the gates
 to post her letter.
 Perhaps when she came back for
5018𓀴dinner she might have
 thought of something
 nice to say, something that
5019𓀴would show Léon she was sorry
 and not aggravate him.
5020𓀴
5021𓀴It was a lovely evening.
 She wandered
 on, seeing at every fresh turn of
5022𓀴the road a yet more
 glorious view.
5023𓀴
5024𓀴The great bay spread
 before her like
 an endless liquid flame. The color
5025𓀴seemed to throb upon
 its burnished shield.
5026𓀴
5027𓀴Naples lay beyond
 it, a long pearly
 circle in the evening light, pale
5028𓀴cream and coral
 pink and soft, dull
 gold. Above Vesuvius the white plume
5029𓀴of smoke drove straight as a
 lifted feather up into the sky.
5030𓀴
5031𓀴She went on till she reached the
 Madonna of the Rocks, then she sat
5032𓀴under the tall raised
 figure with its lamp.
5033𓀴
5034𓀴At the turn of the road below her
 a little carriage was standing; in it
5035𓀴was the figure of
 a woman in white.
 The figure reminded her of Madame
5036𓀴Gérard, only it
 could not be Madame
 Gérard, of course, because Madame
5037𓀴had written to Rose
 that she was not
 well and could not leave her room.
5038𓀴
5039𓀴As Rose sat there
 her eyes filled with
 tears. They were not for herself,
5040𓀴though her own heart was sore;
 they were for the poor woman whose
5041𓀴husband had so cruelly
 left her all
 alone on her honeymoon. And when
5042𓀴Rose thought how happy
 she was herself,
 and how soon she would tell
5043𓀴Léon, with her cheek
 against his cheek,
 that she was sorry she had been
5044𓀴horrid, her heart ached for that
 other bride who had no lover to
5045𓀴appease; and who must be looking
 at all this great sparkling sea and
5046𓀴wonderful bright earth with such
 sad, different eyes! And so Rose sat
5047𓀴there and cried for
 Madame Gérard--and
 Madame Gérard, two hundred yards
5048𓀴away, waited for Rose’s husband.
5049𓀴
5050𓀴He came at last,
 hurriedly, quietly,
 with hanging head, like a thief. He
5051𓀴was ashamed, ashamed
 of his anger against
 Rose, of his incredible folly,
5052𓀴of his silly, intemperate
 desires.
 He passed close by the rock on which
5053𓀴Rose sat. Her heart
 moved suddenly
 against her side; it betrayed her;
5054𓀴stubbornly it beat
 as if it knew itself
 in danger, and yet, Rose said to
5055𓀴herself, there was
 no danger. It was
 only Léon hurrying by, looking as
5056𓀴if he were ashamed.
5057𓀴
5058𓀴She saw him get into
 the little carriage,
 and then turn and look back.
5059𓀴She could not see
 his face, but it
 seemed to her as if he were reluctant
5060𓀴to be driven away. Of course
 he would be back for dinner.
5061𓀴
5062𓀴Perhaps, after all,
 that was Madame
 Gérard, and Léon was driving her
5063𓀴down to the eight o’clock
 boat? Probably
 she was going to Naples to join
5064𓀴her husband, and Léon had offered
 to see her off. He would be very late
5065𓀴for dinner. If she
 hadn’t been cross
 he would have told her what he
5066𓀴meant to do. The
 little Capri ponies
 plunged forward and the carriage
5067𓀴disappeared in a cloud of dust. A
 long while after she saw the little
5068𓀴steamer pushing
 its way across the
 crystal sea and leaving behind it a
5069𓀴long purple trail. She watched
 it till it lost itself beyond
5070𓀴Castellamare. Léon
 would soon be back
 now. She walked slowly towards the
5071𓀴hotel and when she got there she
 was conscious of something strange
5072𓀴about it. The Padrone
 met her with
 a bunch of flowers, and the stout
5073𓀴Padrona bustled
 out from the office
 to ask Rose if there wasn’t anything
5074𓀴extra she would like--would
 she not dine now in the garden?
5075𓀴
5076𓀴“Oh, no, not now,”
 Rose said quickly.
 “I will wait for my husband.” A
5077𓀴shadow passed over
 the Padrona’s face.
 She hesitated and then said with
5078𓀴urgent kindness, “The Signora has
 only to ask for anything she wants.”
5079𓀴The waiter, too, looked at Rose
 with strange, sympathetic eyes. He
5080𓀴suggested her feeding
 the pigeons,
 and hurried to offer her new bread
5081𓀴off the table of some
 traveling Germans.
5082𓀴
5083𓀴“These people,”
 he said, “Tedeschi
 will not know the difference. Take
5084𓀴it, Signora mia, for your birds.”
5085𓀴
5086𓀴The pigeons had already
 gone to roost.
5087𓀴
5088𓀴Peppina, the chambermaid,
 watched Rose
 from the balcony. She should have
5089𓀴been at her supper, but she stood
 for some time gazing down into the
5090𓀴garden at the figure of the young
 wife. Suddenly she also bethought
5091𓀴herself of something
 and hurried down
 into the garden carrying a black
5092𓀴kitten in her apron
 which she deposited
 on Rose’s lap. “Behold,” she
5093𓀴said, “the little one of fortune.
 A black cat brings luck. Talk to it,
5094𓀴Signora, perhaps
 it will stay with
 you.” But the black kitten jumped off
5095𓀴Rose’s lap. It wanted
 to play with
 its own shadow in the grass, and to
5096𓀴stalk birds. It was
 not too young for that.
5097𓀴
5098𓀴The sky changed slowly
 from rose color
 to a clear, pale blue. One by one
5099𓀴the stars came out, but they made
 no place in the sky, till the evening
5100𓀴waned and night came, velvety and
 black, to Capri, embracing it like a
5101𓀴dropped mantle, and then, through
 the curtain of the mysterious dark,
5102𓀴the stars grew enormous and shone
 down upon the scented lemon gardens
5103𓀴and over the vague wide sea.
5104𓀴
5105𓀴Outside the gate
 a mandolin struck
 up a hungry, empty little tune.
5106𓀴
5107𓀴Rose shivered and moved back into
 the house. She could not bear the
5108𓀴beauty of the garden
 any more alone.
5109𓀴
5110𓀴The Padrona met her with a letter
 in her hand. She had had it for two
5111𓀴hours, but she could
 not make up her
 mind to give it to Rose. “How,” she
5112𓀴asked her husband, “am I to slay
 happiness?--I am not a butcher.”
5113𓀴
5114𓀴“Signora,” she said
 nervously, “here
 is a little letter--it is doubtless
5115𓀴from the Signore. He is perhaps
 detained--hospitable
 friends have kept
5116𓀴him--” Rose held out her hand for
 the letter. The Pinsents never made
5117𓀴fusses. They didn’t
 believe in bad
 things happening, and when they
5118𓀴happened they tried to look
 as if they weren’t bad.
5119𓀴
5120𓀴This was the way Rose looked now.
 She smiled pleasantly at the Padrona,
5121𓀴and moved slowly away towards her
 room with the letter. She would not
5122𓀴hurry.
5123𓀴
5124𓀴The Padrona gazed compassionately
 after her. “She is walking over a
5125𓀴precipice,” said the
 Padrona to herself,
 “as if it were a path in our
5126𓀴garden, Poverina!”
5127𓀴
5128𓀴It was a very short letter.
5129𓀴
5130𓀴“My dear,” Léon wrote in French,
 “I find I must go to Naples. It will
5131𓀴not be for long
 I leave you, and I
 have told them all to look after you
5132𓀴until my return.
 Forgive me. Léon.”
5133𓀴
5134𓀴After all he could
 not lie to Rose.
5135𓀴
5136𓀴She read his letter
 three times. The
 first two times she translated his
5137𓀴letter into English,
 and wondered why
 Léon had gone to Naples. The third
5138𓀴time she read it
 without translating
 it, and then she knew everything.
5139𓀴She knew everything
 in all the world.
5140𓀴
5141𓀴But she could not
 quite believe it.
 The arrogance in her rose up and
5142𓀴fought against the truth.
5143𓀴
5144𓀴Rose had very little
 arrogance, but
 all women who have been loved must
5145𓀴have some. Surely
 he who was so much
 her lover could not have left her
5146𓀴so soon?
5147𓀴
5148𓀴She remembered that
 when she had said
 to her mother, “But I could never
5149𓀴leave Léon,” Mrs.
 Pinsent had made
 no direct response. Her mother had
5150𓀴realized that that
 wasn’t the only
 question. How had she realized this?
5151𓀴Had her father ever--?
 Rose buried
 her face in her hands and wept
5152𓀴bitterly. “Oh, poor
 mother!” she murmured,
 “poor mother!” She could not
5153𓀴see herself as wholly poor yet.
5154𓀴
5155𓀴And then she remembered
 Léon’s face
 as he passed her, his sad, ashamed
5156𓀴face, and she knew now why he had
 left her; but that he did not want to
5157𓀴leave her.
5158𓀴
5159𓀴She sat up very straight
 and stopped
 crying when she realized this.
5160𓀴
5161𓀴She thought it very
 strange, for she
 knew quite well that Madame Gérard
5162𓀴didn’t love Léon,
 either. She loved
 her own husband, Rose had seen this;
5163𓀴she knew it as if it were
 in the multiplication
 table; but she couldn’t
5164𓀴think of Madame Gérard now, she
 wasn’t her business. Léon was her
5165𓀴business. She must
 understand, why
 he had done this thing. It wasn’t any
5166𓀴use being silly and just crying,
 then it might happen again, and it
5167𓀴should never happen
 again; she wasn’t
 going to have Léon looking ashamed
5168𓀴twice.
5169𓀴
5170𓀴From the first what
 wrung her heart
 was that Léon would feel it so! He
5171𓀴had meant to be such
 a help, he nearly
 had been, and if he hadn’t been
5172𓀴wasn’t it because Rose had failed
 him? She hadn’t meant to fail him of
5173𓀴course, she had meant
 just the opposite;
 but that was before she knew
5174𓀴all about everything, and before
 you know how to mean, meaning isn’t
5175𓀴going to be much of a help.
5176𓀴
5177𓀴She had thought Léon was strong.
 He wasn’t strong, but in the rush of
5178𓀴her passionate reasoning
 she carried
 this feather-weight of disadvantage
5179𓀴into the fathomless
 sea of her love
 and left it safely there. No, he
5180𓀴wasn’t strong--but he
 was Léon--he was hers.
5181𓀴
5182𓀴It was she who should
 have realized
 his weakness. She remembered now
5183𓀴that once or twice lately he had
 turned back from his excursions with
5184𓀴Madame to suggest
 that Rose should
 join them, but she had refused in her
5185𓀴foolish pride because
 she had wanted
 to prove to him how magnanimous she
5186𓀴was. She shouldn’t have done that
 at all, she shouldn’t have had any
5187𓀴pride--and it didn’t
 matter in the
 least whether she was magnanimous or
5188𓀴not! She should have held him to
 her by whatever could have kept him
5189𓀴there. Tears, if tears
 were necessary;
 pity, duty, pleading--anything
5190𓀴and everything that
 would have helped him.
5191𓀴
5192𓀴She had been thinking of what he
 would think of her--not of what he
5193𓀴needed in her! She
 saw now it only
 mattered what he thought of her in so
5194𓀴far as it helped her to save him.
 Her magnanimity hadn’t saved him.
5195𓀴Something less beautiful but more
 practical might have saved him, her
5196𓀴just being, for instance,
 a little more there.
5197𓀴
5198𓀴But he hadn’t lied
 to her, she came
 back to that as if it was something
5199𓀴on which her heart
 might rest. Ah!
 if he had done that she would have
5200𓀴known that he no
 longer loved her!
5201𓀴
5202𓀴But he had given
 her no reason--no
 excuse; he had flung his sin before
5203𓀴her because he was
 ashamed, because
 he wanted his soul to be naked in
5204𓀴her sight--because he knew
 that she would never fail him.
5205𓀴
5206𓀴In the dark she
 caught sight of the
 hovering Peppina. “Signora,” Peppina
5207𓀴pleaded, “will you not dine?”
5208𓀴
5209𓀴Rose stood up. “Yes,” she said in
 a voice that sounded strange. “Yes,
5210𓀴please, I will dine.”
5211𓀴
5212𓀴The Pinsents always dined.
5213𓀴
5214𓀴“Tell the Padrona,”
 Rose said steadily,
 “that the Signore has had to go
5215𓀴to Naples on business. He
 will not return to-night.”
5216𓀴
5217𓀴Peppina still hovered.
 “Si Signora,”
 she said, “and the black cat, the
5218𓀴one I brought to
 the Signora earlier
 in the evening, he has found for
5219𓀴himself the room of
 the Signora. Behold,
 he lies there curled-up on her
5220𓀴bed. He is there
 now--a miracle! The
 Signora remembers that I told her
5221𓀴‘a black cat means
 good fortune’?”
5222𓀴
5223𓀴Rose hurried into
 the room, and found
 him. He was not quite so good as
5224𓀴her fox terrier at home, but he
 was a comfort. She buried her cheek
5225𓀴against the round black ball of
 the fortunate kitten, and wept with
5226𓀴easier tears.
5227𓀴
5228𓀴Then she went down and had
 her dinner in the garden.
5229𓀴
5230𓀴
5231𓀴                   
           CHAPTER XIII
5232𓀴
5233𓀴They sat on a terrace overlooking
 the most beautiful view in the world.
5234𓀴They did not look at it, nor did
 they look at each other. They were
5235𓀴beautifully dressed,
 they lived in
 the same world and spoke the same
5236𓀴tongue; they would
 have laughed at,
 if they would not have made, the
5237𓀴same jokes. The materials
 for happiness
 were heaped before them; but
5238𓀴neither of them stretched
 out a hand
 to take them. They were both like
5239𓀴creatures under an invisible ban.
5240𓀴
5241𓀴It could not be
 said that Léon had
 any cause for a grievance. Madame
5242𓀴Gérard had given him what she had
 offered him, but he had fatally
5243𓀴under-estimated how far this gift
 would fall short of what he wanted.
5244𓀴
5245𓀴From the moment
 of their departure
 from Capri it had come over him that
5246𓀴Elise was not beautiful, that she
 had no particular charm of person nor
5247𓀴of mind; she neither touched nor
 soothed him. There was a fatal
5248𓀴alteration in her.
 She was accessible.
5249𓀴
5250𓀴Léon could not tell
 what had caused
 this change in his feelings--he had
5251𓀴been covered so lightly
 by a rare and
 perfect tenderness that he had not
5252𓀴realized how it warmed
 and nourished
 him, until he found himself sharply
5253𓀴deprived of it.
5254𓀴
5255𓀴He felt like some
 one suddenly pushed
 into the dark. He fumbled and
5256𓀴knocked himself
 against obstacles,
 possessed by an intolerable fear, a
5257𓀴fear that he shouldn’t
 get out, shouldn’t
 ever get back into his light
5258𓀴again. He knew now what the light
 was, he had been in relation to
5259𓀴perfect purity, and it was not
 until the relation ceased that he
5260𓀴realized it had not left
 him as it found him.
5261𓀴
5262𓀴He no longer wanted
 anything less.
 He wanted only his flawless jewel,
5263𓀴the deep and incorruptible
 heart of
 Rose. And as for the first time he
5264𓀴knew the hunger of a real desire,
 he knew also that he shrank from
5265𓀴returning to her after
 so light and base a sin.
5266𓀴
5267𓀴He had thought this
 three days could
 be nothing, an episode, a chance
5268𓀴wayside plucking of
 a flower, something
 that he could quite easily put
5269𓀴away from him and forget
 on his return to Rose.
5270𓀴
5271𓀴He now discovered
 that it would burn
 into his heart like a corrosive
5272𓀴fluid, and make him fear to seek
 her presence. It was not that he
5273𓀴doubted Rose would forgive him;
 but he came up against something in
5274𓀴himself which would
 not yield forgiveness.
 He had too easily gone wrong.
5275𓀴
5276𓀴He kept his eyes
 carefully away from
 Madame Gérard. He hated her with a
5277𓀴cold antagonism;
 he could not make
 love to her. He fell back on a
5278𓀴sharpened irony of
 attention. She should
 have all that she wanted and he
5279𓀴waited upon her
 with an exaggerated
 courtesy; but she was as oblivious
5280𓀴of his coldness as she
 had been of his warmth.
5281𓀴
5282𓀴Léon had never known
 so strange a woman.
5283𓀴
5284𓀴As for Madame Gérard,
 she had effected
 her purpose. Last night at the
5285𓀴Opera, seated in
 the front of a box
 with Léon beside her, she had caught
5286𓀴and held the eyes of
 her enraged husband.
 That was what she had come to
5287𓀴Naples for.
5288𓀴
5289𓀴Léon had not seen
 him. Monsieur Gérard
 sufficiently accompanied to feel
5290𓀴that a scene would have been out
 of place, had swiftly withdrawn.
5291𓀴
5292𓀴But before he had withdrawn, his
 eyes had crossed swords with his
5293𓀴wife’s.
5294𓀴
5295𓀴After that there
 seemed very little
 to do. She was conscious that the
5296𓀴rest of her life lay before her,
 and that her husband would never
5297𓀴forgive her. The prospect once
 accepted, ceased to stimulate.
5298𓀴
5299𓀴From time to time
 she was conscious
 of Léon, but never as a
5300𓀴consideration requiring
 much effort.
 She had fulfilled her bargain and
5301𓀴nothing more seemed
 to be asked of
 her. She felt with relief that rather
5302𓀴less was required
 of her than might
 have been expected, and she was
5303𓀴vaguely grateful to Léon for
 leaving her so much alone.
5304𓀴
5305𓀴He was a man of tact
 and could be trusted
 to look out her trains for her
5306𓀴and see her eventually
 back to France.
 She supposed she would have
5307𓀴sooner or later to
 rejoin her parents;
 but she wished she could forget
5308𓀴what she had done to Rose.
5309𓀴
5310𓀴Now that her purpose
 was accomplished
 this fact became more and more
5311𓀴troublesome to her. Léon she had
 no qualms about, for she realized
5312𓀴neither his unhappiness
 nor what she
 had cost him, but she did realize
5313𓀴Rose.
5314𓀴
5315𓀴It made her a little
 sharp with Léon
 when she thought of him at all; but
5316𓀴it was quite easy
 not to think of him.
5317𓀴
5318𓀴Madame Gérard wanted to ask him
 if he had succeeded in keeping Rose
5319𓀴unaware, but she
 shrank from speaking
 of Rose. Neither of them spoke of
5320𓀴her, and neither
 of them thought of
 anything else. It made the silence
5321𓀴heavy between them.
5322𓀴
5323𓀴“You would like something to eat
 or drink, perhaps?” Léon at length
5324𓀴roused himself to ask her.
 “No,” she said, “thank you.”
5325𓀴
5326𓀴He lit a cigarette and smoked it
 through, then he said, “It is, I
5327𓀴believe, considered
 very beautiful
 to drive to Posilippo in the
5328𓀴sunset--to dine out
 there and return.
 Shall I order a carriage?”
5329𓀴
5330𓀴She turned her head
 for a brief moment
 and glanced at him. She wished he
5331𓀴would go away now--drive
 to Posilippo
 by himself, for instance. “Do as
5332𓀴you like,” she said
 without stirring,
 “I stay here--” “Then, of course,”
5333𓀴he said gravely, “I shall not
 leave you.” It was like being in
5334𓀴prison--and not being quite sure
 whether you were the prisoner or the
5335𓀴jailer.
5336𓀴
5337𓀴It was a relief to know that some
 one else was advancing along the
5338𓀴terrace. Léon sprang to his feet;
 he was not a clumsy man, but he very
5339𓀴nearly upset the table
 by which they sat.
5340𓀴
5341𓀴Rose was walking
 slowly towards them.
 She held a Baedeker in one hand
5342𓀴and a parasol in
 the other. She was
 very tall, and she looked taller
5343𓀴than usual. Her wide
 blue eyes rested
 on the wonderful sea beyond--but
5344𓀴she had seen Léon
 and Madame Gérard.
 She walked towards them without
5345𓀴speaking or smiling.
5346𓀴
5347𓀴When she came up
 to them she smiled
 a little nervously, but in a very
5348𓀴friendly way, as
 if she was glad to
 see them both, but didn’t want, of
5349𓀴course, to make a fuss about it.
5350𓀴
5351𓀴“They told me,” she said, “that
 I should find you out here.”
5352𓀴
5353𓀴Madame Gérard could not rise. Her
 lips moved as if she tried to speak,
5354𓀴but she dared not
 speak. This was her
 judgment. She was the cleverest of
5355𓀴women, but she no longer
 knew what to say.
5356𓀴
5357𓀴Léon stood there with his eyes on
 the ground, white as a sheet and
5358𓀴trembling. He could
 not look at Rose
 at all. He felt as if her eyes were
5359𓀴fire from Heaven.
5360𓀴
5361𓀴Rose spoke again.
 “Léon,” she said,
 “do you think I might have some
5362𓀴tea?”
5363𓀴
5364𓀴“_Mon Dieu_--Rose--” he whispered
 under his breath. “_Mon Dieu_--what
5365𓀴must you think--”
5366𓀴
5367𓀴“If I could have some
 roll and butter,
 too,” she went on, ignoring his
5368𓀴murmur, “it would be very nice. I
 am rather hungry.” Léon turned and
5369𓀴without speaking
 passed quickly into
 the house. Rose sat down opposite
5370𓀴Madame and put the
 Baedeker on the
 table. Madame Gérard lifted her heavy
5371𓀴eyelids and looked at Rose.
5372𓀴
5373𓀴She did not know what was coming,
 but she meant whatever came--scorn,
5374𓀴anger or contempt--to take it.
5375𓀴
5376𓀴She was not sure what Rose
 wanted--she waited to be sure.
5377𓀴
5378𓀴Rose met her eyes
 with a grave and
 infinitely kindly look. “I am so
5379𓀴sorry,” she said slowly
 in her hesitating
 French. “We meant to help you,
5380𓀴but I’m afraid we didn’t.”
5381𓀴
5382𓀴Madame drew a quick breath, she
 had not expected this. It had not
5383𓀴occurred to her
 that Rose would be
 sorry; that hard, stubborn substance
5384𓀴that was in her
 breast melted once
 and for all towards Rose. The tears
5385𓀴filled her eyes and fell
 slowly into her lap.
5386𓀴
5387𓀴“My dear,” she said,
 “no one could
 help me, and I have not even--helped
5388𓀴myself.”
5389𓀴
5390𓀴“I was stupid,” Rose
 went on gently,
 “and I didn’t understand; but I do
5391𓀴understand now. What
 I wanted to say
 before Léon comes back was, that I
5392𓀴_know_ he meant
 not to make things
 worse. You _will_ forgive him, won’t
5393𓀴you, because it was
 my fault really.
 If I had understood, you see, I
5394𓀴should have known
 he couldn’t help
 you--not in that way--and I think I
5395𓀴could have stopped him.”
5396𓀴
5397𓀴Madame Gérard nodded.
 “I have nothing
 to forgive your husband,” she
5398𓀴said, choosing her
 words carefully.
 “He has done me no great wrong;
5399𓀴always I knew where
 his heart was--it
 is still there, Madame--it is in
5400𓀴your hands. I--”
 said Madame Gérard,
 looking away from Rose’s pitying,
5401𓀴tender eyes--“have what
 I deserve. I have nothing.”
5402𓀴
5403𓀴The waiter came
 with the tea. Léon
 returned at the same time. He could
5404𓀴not keep away, and
 yet it seemed to
 him as if there had never been less
5405𓀴of him anywhere--his
 self-respect,
 his manhood had left him.
5406𓀴
5407𓀴Rose turned to him,
 and with a little
 gesture of perfect tenderness and
5408𓀴trust she slipped
 her hand over his.
 It was as if she gave him back his
5409𓀴soul. He drew himself
 up--strength
 passed into him. She had come back,
5410𓀴she was his--somehow
 or other she was
 there to save him, and at least he
5411𓀴could be generous--he
 could let himself
 be saved. He no longer cared
5412𓀴that he must be a
 poor figure in her
 sight, and he forgot that there was
5413𓀴any other sight but hers.
5414𓀴
5415𓀴She withdrew her
 hand again and went
 on very slowly, still in French,
5416𓀴including him in the conversation
 with a little wave of the hand.
5417𓀴
5418𓀴“I have just,” she said to Madame
 Gérard, “been talking to Monsieur
5419𓀴Gérard. He thinks I have improved
 very much in my French.”
5420𓀴
5421𓀴“My husband!” Madame
 cried, starting
 forward, then she sank back,
5422𓀴white-lipped and trembling.
5423𓀴
5424𓀴“Yes,” said Rose, “I went to see
 him. I found him in the Baedeker. He
5425𓀴was in the sixth
 hotel I called at.”
5426𓀴
5427𓀴“But why,” began
 Madame Gérard, “why
 did you seek him--Madame, what did
5428𓀴you say to him? Forgive
 me, I do not understand?”
5429𓀴
5430𓀴“I thought perhaps
 I had better see
 him first,” Rose explained. “I saw
5431𓀴him in the hall.
 I think he was in
 a kind of rage--he said he had seen
5432𓀴you last night at a theater with
 Léon, and I said, yes--that I never
5433𓀴went to theaters in Italy because
 I didn’t understand the language, and
5434𓀴then he asked me if I had
 been with you all the time.”
5435𓀴
5436𓀴Madame Gérard held her breath.
 Her eyes seemed like a prayer.
5437𓀴
5438𓀴Rose turned to Léon.
 “I’m afraid I
 didn’t tell him the truth,” she said
5439𓀴hesitatingly. “I
 hope it wasn’t very
 dreadful--I said, yes, of course I
5440𓀴had.”
5441𓀴
5442𓀴“You lied to him!” gasped
 Léon. “Then--then--”
 for the first time he
5443𓀴looked at Madame
 Gérard. She covered
 her face with her hands. Rose
5444𓀴looked a little perturbed.
 “I didn’t
 know,” she said, “what else there
5445𓀴was to be done. Of course I know
 it was very wrong. I never have been
5446𓀴untruthful before.
 I--I don’t like
 telling lies, but I thought--I’d
5447𓀴better. So I said
 we were all together.
 I was a little afraid he
5448𓀴mightn’t believe me or that he
 might ask me where we were, but he
5449𓀴didn’t. He quite
 believed me. He only
 asked me what I wanted to see him
5450𓀴for.”
5451𓀴
5452𓀴“_Par exemple_,” muttered
 Léon; “he asked you that?”
5453𓀴
5454𓀴Rose poured herself
 out a second cup
 of tea. “I said,” she went on, “I
5455𓀴came because I thought you might
 be sorry for leaving your wife all
5456𓀴alone--just because
 she tried to turn
 over your music for you--and that
5457𓀴I thought perhaps
 you might be wanting
 to tell her so--and not know
5458𓀴where she was.”
5459𓀴
5460𓀴Madame’s eyes fell from her face.
 “But yes--” she whispered, “and what
5461𓀴did he say, Raoul, when
 you asked him that?”
5462𓀴
5463𓀴There was a new look in her eyes
 now, and a little color in her pale
5464𓀴cheeks.
5465𓀴
5466𓀴“He said he _was_
 sorry,” said Rose,
 gently. “He said he never would
5467𓀴have behaved like that, and never
 meant to--it was only the music, he
5468𓀴said, he often lost his head over
 music, and that that afternoon he had
5469𓀴felt how great a
 success his marriage
 was--so that it was doubly
5470𓀴unfortunate. He said he wanted
 to come back to you very much.”
5471𓀴
5472𓀴There was a moment’s
 pause. Madame
 Gérard’s voice was quite different
5473𓀴when she spoke now,
 there was hope
 in it. “And what answer did you give
5474𓀴him, Madame?” she said. “I think
 I can see by your eyes that you gave
5475𓀴him an answer.”
5476𓀴
5477𓀴Rose nodded. “I told him--I had
 a feeling that you would forgive
5478𓀴him--and that I would ask you,
 if you did, to send him a line
5479𓀴to-night--saying if you would see
 him, and where, of course! You see I
5480𓀴didn’t know where you were at the
 time--but I found you quite easily,
5481𓀴because I had remembered
 something
 that Léon had said to me about this
5482𓀴special view.”
5483𓀴
5484𓀴Léon buried his head
 in his hands and
 laughed wildly. He laughed to save
5485𓀴himself from tears.
 Madame Gérard said
 nothing at all; but she stretched
5486𓀴out her hand for the tea Rose had
 poured out for her and began to drink
5487𓀴it.
5488𓀴
5489𓀴Rose ate two rolls
 and a half. “I’m
 afraid you’ll think I’m dreadfully
5490𓀴greedy,” she explained,
 “but I haven’t
 had any lunch, or any breakfast
5491𓀴either, properly.”
5492𓀴
5493𓀴“But I,” said Léon, coming from
 behind his hands, “I cannot meet
5494𓀴Monsieur Gérard to-morrow?”
5495𓀴
5496𓀴“No,” said Rose, “but
 I left my luggage
 on the Quay. There is a boat
5497𓀴that goes to Venice to-night, and
 I thought,” she murmured with a
5498𓀴diffident, disarming smile, “that
 perhaps you wouldn’t mind if we just
5499𓀴went to Venice, Léon.
 It would be more gay.”
5500𓀴
5501𓀴
5502𓀴                 
               THE END
5503𓀴
